RATING: 6
[I]tl;dr Star Realms is a decent deckbuilder with a peripheral sci-fi theme but great interactions. The authority track is an especially great addition to the game.[/I]
[COLOR=#CCCCFF]____________________________[/COLOR]

I was introduced to Star Realms after my father-in-law picked up Colony Wars at GenCon and wanted to introduce me to it. He's logged hundreds of plays against AI opponents, so I knew I would get blown away, but it presented another chance to learn a deck-builder, so I took that chance. 

In Star Realms, you are trying to buy ships and bases to activate powerful actions that will let you, well, buy more ships and bases. Like most deckbuilders, there's a lot of repetitiveness in Star Realms that can get old. However, I did appreciate the game's main point: siphoning the opponent's influence down to 0. This makes the game feel incredibly personal and strategy choices have far-reaching effects. 

Timing is everything. I was able to beat Tim down to only 4 points at one time, but his superior cards and VP-generating capabilities let him quickly recharge and proceed to steamroll me. If I had collected myself a little better, I could have made that one big push more efficiently. This optimization part of the game definitely appeals to me, since I'm prone to be excessively tactical and optimize individual turns, without a longer-term strategy. Star Realms forces you to do otherwise. 

Since playing with more players, I can say that the strategy shifts with four players as you're trying to beat other players down but eventually just finish one or more of them off. We found that we'd generate ridiculous game-ending combos off of each other and shut each other down. So, like most deckbuilders, this ends abruptly. 

Ended up trading it, didn't do it for me.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Star Realms imitates Dominion but is designed for two players only. However, Star Realms is inferior by almost every aspect. In Dominion you at least get the satisfaction of analyzing the setup, deciding on a strategy, building your deck and seeing your game plan come to life. None of this exists in Star Realms, though. You cannot have a strategy, since you just have to choose from the cards drawn from the deck. All plays are made at the tactical level without a long term plan. Moreover, the round has a tendency to end even before your marvelously built deck has shown its crafty potential. You do not get a chance to show your cunning. All you need is just a small deck of cards that deal a good amount of damage, and the game is over in an eye blink. You do not even want to buy any further cards because it just slows down your fast rotation of cards.
 
A poor game, trying to take a fresh approach to deck building genre by ditching strategy, most of decision making, and the thrill of building your deck. Honestly, I cannot understand the appeal of this game. If you like tactical level games that end quick and can be played without giving the game much of a thought, Star Realms might be your cup of tea. I just find Dominion more appealing offering something for both, the strategists and casual players. 

And it is not even that I despised tactical games, on the contrary, I love many of them. But it is just the fact that after buying a few cards, you just dash for the finish line without doing any more decisions.

RATING: 8
The best deckbuilder in my opinion.  It elegantly sidesteps all the problems that usually bug me in the genre.  This is a game where you MUST build a deck that makes effective combos of cards to do well, but unlike other deckbuilders, a careful player can almost always do so, and none of the mechanics of the game fight against your ability to build the deck you want.  The tradeoff is that it really only works with two players (I'm not a fan of the multi-player variants, most lifted from Magic, detailed in the rules), but I usually play this type of game with two anyway.  One of very few games that I'm pretty much always up for playing.

RATING: 5
I haven't played many card engine building games so I cannot compare them to each other.

The positives for me: science fiction theme, fast play, highly competitive, attack cards, cool combo cards.

The negatives for me: the scoring cards are a PITA (although you can come up with your own scoring system I suppose). Variability relies on expansions (= $). Fells like I am playing the same game over and over again by which I mean it feels like a re-themed Dominion. That's a not a bad thing and I am probably a little off with the comparison but the feeling remains.

I'd play an app or computer version but don't think I'd play the card version all that often.

So 7 for the app version and 5 for the card version leaves me a 6.

RATING: 8
I got the decks and then bought the app.  I have way over 2,000 plays on it and this is one fabulous game.  You can play it in about 20 minutes: 5 on a phone.  It is a challenging with a lot of strategy in a small package.
Don't believe the noise about luck of the draw and obvious choices.  If you think that is true, get the phone app and play the A.I. on the difficult level.  You have to know the value of the cards beyond their cost and how they fit into your deck.  You have to develop a strategy, as chaining same-faction cards is extremely powerful and each one has its advantages.  You have to realize that sometimes you shouldn't spend "money" that you have to acquire ships, just because it is there, as deck-cloggers can kill you.  You also have to know just when to trash a card and take the chance that you can pull off the win.
Greatest filler I have ever played: hands down!

RATING: 2
There were more choices than I thought - but just as little control over the outcome. 

Effectively, this is a game about pacing - you try to either ramp up your spending to acquire more efficient threats or you cash in and go into all out attack mode. Unfortunately, the 'drafting' row - of only five cards - doesn't allow you to craft much strategy, and quite frankly, doesn't even allow you to be positive tactically enough of the time. 

I think Ascension, online, was worth some play to learn the limitations of a fun set, but this is the same deal, but the online implementation is weaker. 

The shuffling would be a big problem if I played this in real life.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Star Realms is a highly engaging deck-building and drafting game with very few flaws that should replace the bulk of your existing deckbuilders.

QUALITY:
The card quality is serviceable for a few games but quickly wears down to the point of needing sleeves. The game is fairly cheap, so it feels a bit weird spending upwards $5 to sleeve a $10 game.

ART/UI:
The UI by Vito Gesualdi is pretty good in some places. The symbology makes sense, is easy to remember; the body typography choice of Handel Gothic was classy, and the colors have good pop and compliment the art. I would nitpick that the overuse of beveling does detract from the card design and makes it a bit tackier.

The art is pretty superb in most cases. You have a few instances where cards are clearly just a bunch of separate pieces thrown together in Photoshop with glows and filters added. However, most of the art is top-notch spacefaring stuff with lots of added color to keep you engaged and keep cards looking memorable.

GAMEPLAY:
Gameplay consists heavily of drafting. This will be roughly the only decision you ever make in the game: which of these 5 cards do I take (that I can actually afford)? Tons of incredibly complex game situations spring forward from just this one simple starting point. You can talk for hours about how many "levels" of nuanced play a player can learn after each game, such as knowing to pick up scrap cards to thin the deck, scrapping starting Viper cards first, knowing when to loop your deck and when not to, knowing what cards will make it through less deck loops, and knowing how to both create your own damage trajectory as well as counter-draft an opponent. There's a lot more that goes into the game that wouldn't be appropriate for a review, so let's just say that it has an insane level of depth that you could be exploring for a year or more of solid play.

THE BAD STUFF:
Every game has a flaw and Star Realms' is randomness. No matter how good at the game you get, an opponent getting a Trade Bot in open is going to set you up for a huge disadvantage. If cards bottomdeck (appear on the bottom, thus getting to use them one less time) uncontrollably too often, you can lose through no fault of your own. Between two highly skilled players, much of the gameplay comes down to who went first (first player has an ENORMOUS statistical advantage) and what picks were available on the draft conveyor. Even when you do everything in your power to control the game by limiting your buys and crunching numbers for hours to learn the best choices, the game can just take a fat dump on you for a loss. The only upside to the randomness in the game is that you should almost always be able to beat someone of a lower skill level. However, expect closer skilled players to have a lot less fun, especially ones that hit the skill ceiling of the game. Once you hit that ceiling, the game feels like pure random chance.

VALUE:
You get at least a solid years' worth of play in a $10 or so package. That's some intense value you shouldn't pass up even if you dislike the genre. I don't think I've seen a better value game in board gaming history.

CONCLUSION:
Star Realms has to be one of the tightest, most engaging, most highly replayable games I have ever played. It is nearly perfect in game design and looks decently sleek as well. This straight replaces several other games in its own genre. The only reason I can't give this a 10 on BGG is that the rating system precludes any game which I would stop loving and once you hit that skill ceiling, you stop loving just seeing randomness be the dominant factor.

Buy the game anyway.

RATING: 7
A good, strong, easy to learn game. Everyone seems to enjoy it, that I have seen.

The only demerits are:

1. While battle adds a slight element of interaction between players, it's pretty easy to play with a mindset of "well whatever's on the board when it's my turn is just what's on the board". It doesn't really feel like you're competing against someone else because fundamentally you just buy whatever's best in the market and attack as you can. The game doesn't really allow for a player to create strategies, such that you would need to care what the other player is doing and react to break it. As said, you just buy what's best and move on.

2. If I remember right, the rules have you down from 20 HP. At that level, who wins is basically just luck and the game ends before you've barely even got started. I recommend playing 2 players to 70 HP each (we always just shortcutted it and started with 68, ignoring the first two damage).

RATING: 9
Yes. Yes. We know. Another deck builder? And a sci-fi themed one at that? Indeed. This one gets high marks for being a simplistic game of attack and recruit. "Core Worlds for Dummies" if you will. We tried Core Worlds before and were totally gob-smacked with how mathy (nope not a word either but you get the idea) it is. The game should come with a calculator. But Star Realms takes the concept of building up forces in your armada/system to attack your adversary and makes it so simple it's almost obscene. LOL. That simplicity and ease of play is what puts it on the same level with Eminent Domain. We don't think you can go wrong with this one.

RATING: 2
This game is very very very very average. 75% luck, 15% good fortune, 2% strategy (but ranked 50 odd on stategy list lol) & 8%....... er......... well I guess you could call it 'skill' but only if you threaten to punch me in the throat. Lets call it 'correct decision making' ability. If thats your idea of fun buy this game. The one major advantage it has its that is generally so obvious, after 12-14 turns who is going to win, if you are playing with a friend who knows the game well, one of you can concede and you get get through 8-16 games in an hour. Sure every now and again (1 in 5-6) you might be able to pull of a win despite this but really what the point? Usually that's often down to terrible/great card draws etc. The decision making is so obvious you'd have to be a brain dead mouse not to figure the best option 90% of the time. Very rarely you'll get 'tricky' choices but they are rare. I suspect if you played the app game and asked the company to provide you with a computer AI copy and a simple modification, you could get a heavy book to play the game for you and simply make the odd obvious decision that's required. The game feels often like your along for the rid, whether you win or lose and the game is playing you rather than the other way round. I have to hand it to the game designers though to provide the illusion of choice and tactical decisions for people whilst it sreally a very simply 75-90% luck fest, is pure genius. Wish I'd though of it. All in all I just dont get how this game is rated so high but I guess we all have blind spots.

RATING: 6.5
There was a time I really enjoyed Star Realms, so much so I played the card version, the iPhone version, etc.  But, the more I played, the more I started to see that it was truly a game that rewards experience.  Now, what game doesn't, right?  But, with this game, I felt like if you took a casual approach to it and attempted to play with a more serious gamer, you're toast.  I saw this a lot with a gaming partner of mine who played(s) this non-stop on his phone.  I took a break for a while and picked it back up.  He destroyed me.  I feel like there are paths to victory that make themselves evident with time.  Sadly, this disparity has decreased my overall opinion of the game for this reason.  Perhaps selfish, but it's my review.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.9
Star Realms really surprised me. I had only heard it in passing in a Rahdo review before someone bringing out their set at Kingdom Con. I love Dominion & deck builders and always am willing to try a new one. I thought that this felt like a lot of games already, like Ascension with its multiple currencies and how you buy new cards from the common pool pulled off the big deck and Legendary with its chaining of hero power activations. The life total and attacking people directly with one of the currencies felt fresh enough that it stood out. The game also has space stations which act as buffers that have to be attacked first or add special abilities. All in all I had a great time and really enjoyed the multiplayer variants. We did 2-headed (2 on 2) and a variant where you only attacked to the right; both lots of fun. The game is also cheap enough (~$16?) that I'll definitely be picking up a copy. My criticisms is that the number of ships available often feel very limiting where there is typically only one or two real choices to be made and later in the game your deck is mostly playing itself as you draw (which is a criticism of any deckbuilder but I felt it here more than most). This automation may be worse with repeated plays, but I definitely had fun while I was playing it.

RATING: 8
2/4/16:  Need to try some of the solitaire varients described in this geeklist:  https://boardgamegeek.com/geeklist/189611/star-realms-solo-challenges.

11/22/14: Deckbuilding card game.  This is Ascension set in a Sci-fi world.  The 3 different resources are attack points, purchase points and victory points.  You start with 50 victory points and first one to go to (or below?) zero loses.  In essence a built in countdown.  

Fairly quick playing.   A big bonus is that unlike 20 dollar card games you can purchase a 2 player deck for $10.  The downside is that the deck box will disintegrate quickly. 

I think the playing time is longer than 20 minutes, but we'll see when I have more plays.  Plays excellently with 2, haven't tried it with more.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Star Realms is another take at the deckbuilding genre by the same designers of Ascension.

It shares many things with its elder relative: a variable and ever changing row of cards to acquire, the presence of just two "currencies" (money for purchasing and military to attack), the powers triggered by playing cards of the same faction.

What is different, then? The answer is simple: it is a bloody, fierce, fast and furious duel! Each player struggles to knock off the opponent, by reducing his life from 50 to 0.

So, unlike Ascension you don't build military to defeat monsters from the center row, but to outright crush your opponent.

Another difference lies in bases: they're permanent assets (well, permanent until destroyed, anyway!) capable to provide resources during many turns, and also give players some protection (outposts).

All considered, it's an improvement on Ascension (even if the iOS app is worst than the Ascension app), with a more streamlined design, and much more tension. And it plays definitely faster!

RATING: 7.6
Game Time: 30-45 Mins
Players: 2-4 (Best 2)

Primarily a 2 player deckbuilder with a space theme and 4 primary factions that offer varying playstyles. Factions can be mixed however some bonuses are only achieved when playing cards of the same faction. Win by reducing your opponents influence to 0.

Additional Resources:
Rescue Run(Promo)

Assault
Command
Heroes
Missions
Gambit Set
Crisis - Bases & Battleships
Crisis - Events
Crisis - Fleets & Fortresses
Crisis - Heroes
Storage Box

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a solid, easy to learn deck builder with a fast setup time. It's focused on combat so should be welcomed by those who think Dominion (to which Star Realms owes its roots) is multiplayer solitaire, although it should be noted that Star Realms involves a lot more luck than Dominion.

The game isn't very thinky. If a card is strong or belongs to a faction for which you already own a few cards and you can afford it, you generally want to buy it. And when it comes into play, there's not too much to think about. Resources are money, attack power, and defence (healing). 

The game is fast and pretty fun, but it's not deep.

RATING: 7
Rating based off the pc app but the score will be applied for the physical version.

First.People complaining about the luck.Get over it.Its a card game.

I had always problem with deck building games.For me deckbuilders are just a luckfest and not entertaining at all since the deckbuilding mechanic bores me to death. Even if you try to put more gameplay mechanics around it{like thunderstone}it makes the game even incredible long and the setup times are even longer becouse you need more cards,boards or even dice.

After my bad experiences with thunderstone.Better but not cheap legendary and easy to teach mechanicaly swift dominion{which i liked the most}.Well here comes star realms.

Cheap little addicting PVP deckbuilder in a little portable box.For my friends this will be a huge beer&pretzel game for the pub probably.
We dont need freaking big overproduced boxes for a deckbuilder.I dont think that the mechanics are so huge.
Star realms i hope will start a new trend in this portable deckbuilders.Cheap,small,more PVP,quick and still a lot enjoyable.

EDIT: lowering my hyped score 9 to 7.still too much luck for my taste.sitting and waiting for the big lucky combo fo half life.This is the last deckbuilder for me.

still i cant get lower with this for its pricepoint.

RATING: 9.5
This game has steadily grown on me. Maybe it was an 8 early on. Now it's a 10. I just can't get enough of the game. I find it fun and very, very fun when you trigger multiple cards into play and build up a massive attack. Also there is more strategy to this than meets the eye. For a while, it was a game of luck that I always lost at. I have had to raise my game. I like that you have plenty of quick decisions and that it plays fast, with no AP. I like the expansions too. Really, I am happy to play the base game endlessly, but it's fun to freshen things up with Colony Wars (I want to try a blend with some core, some Colony) and the various other things. I like that Gambit can be used to give a handicap system. Simply give more gambits to whoever needs a helping hand. Heroes I like the idea that you can use them to trigger faction abilities on single cards. Just a really great, play any time, any where game, that also has a very addictive app. For the price, it is a remarkable game. On the minus, to players who do not like take that, it can feel like relentless attack. Other than that, a home run for me.

- Red is deck thinner
- Yellow is deck cycling/discard
- Green is for massive damages, cycling (secondary)
- Blue is hybrid for healing and cash and bases

- Red/Yellow is good with any colour

- Freighter (blue) can be surprising good early/mid-game to buy expensive stuff
- Explorer is pretty good early-game for buying expensive stuff and mid-game to cycle damage when one has nothing to buy (and doesn't want to open for opponent).
- Recycling station is good in event of a bad hand

Initial Impressions:
-----------------------

Lasting Impressions:
-----------------------
There is zero reason to play Ascension anymore, as this game just beats it down in every way.  For a 30-45 game for 2players, I cannot think of many games I'd rather play.  This is a game I will be playing 5 years from now, and  something I will continue to play whenever I get the chance.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Fast setup. Quick play. Exciting decisions, intense gameplay, easy rules. It's missing some more strategy to be a perfect 10.

On the other hand there's a bad balance between efficiency of push-your-luck card building and generally efficient cards, i.e. push your luck strategies are strong, but too unreliable. This is especially true for counter-strategies, that depend on the fact that you're allowed to buy certain cards, which deepen your core strategy. My problem with the game is that the efficiency of buys depends on the future availability of certain combo cards, which when drawn might win you the game. For example if you pursue a blob rush strategy your winning chance scales with the appearance of certain cards and the possibility for the other player to counter you depends on that as well. This leads to some frustrating games, where choosing the correct tactics and strategy still results in a clear loss.

The "solution" is to not rely on any such strategies and instead buy cards, which are strong in general. Those cards will be bought immediately, when they turn up and you can't influence who gets those (unless they're particularly expensive), which is ... frustrating.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Star Realms is a great example of being perfect for what it is.  It's designed to be a 20 minute exercise in deck building, and it accomplishes that perfectly.  The art is great, and the game space is just large enough that the four factions feel unique.  The game focuses almost exclusively on the choice of what to buy - or not buy, as the case may be.  Keeping your deck lean and focused is the challenge.  The deck then pretty much plays itself, and I think this is where the game gets a lot of criticism.  Detractors want meaningful decisions in how they play the cards, and that's just not present (with the exception of when to use scrap abilities).

Star Realms is a fantastic buy at the price point, and worthy of being in every collection as a short 2 player filler.  It's not a brain burner, and it's not meant to be.  It offers a light and short game that is accessible and fun.

I bought Star Realms to play with my 7 year old, and he loves it.  It has quickly become his favorite game.  He plays fairly well, and although I sometimes have to point out how to optimize his hand, he has already figured out how to guide his deck with his purchases.  I highly recommend Star Realms for smaller gamers.

RATING: 6.5
Deckbuilding for dummies. Not that that's necessarily a bad thing, but since the game is over in 10-15 minutes, there are very few long-term strategic choices to make. Luck is also a huge factor here, with some players happening to get a couple of good combo-creating cards which they can then use and reuse until they win. I think I'd probably like the game more if it was played to 100 points (rather than 50). 

As others have said, the scoring cards (with different values on each side) are a nightmare. They are incredibly confusing and present a huge risk for game-changing scoring errors. Use poker chips instead.

:thumbsup: [b]POSITIVE POINTS:[/b]

++ Nice compact deck-building game, that have direct players interaction.
++ Good replayability from the different card that show up in the trade row and from the 4 different factions that have different style of play. Every games, you can set up a new strategy using the different combinaisons of cards available.
++ Good card graphic design, that is clean and clear.
++ Really fast to set-up and super fast to play.
++ There is several expansions available for this game (which is useful to refresh the game when we have overplayed it).


:thumbsdown: [b]NEGATIVE POINTS:[/b]

-- Since this game have an exponential game speed curve (it's maybe too fast), it seem that if you have a bad start (too slow to acquire the right cards or to thin your deck), you already know you will be loosing and winning just past the half game (so it's a clear runaway leader problem here), which is sad. But since I'm playing this game exclusively with the app, it's so fast that it doesn't matter me much.
-- The replayability of this game is lower when always played against the same opponent (as it would be in my case) as part of the variability is how the players play the game and what strategy they try.
-- The game start to feel samey quickly too, even if you haven't try all the possible combinaison of factions or card combos yet.
-- The space theme, the direct player interaction and the fact that I already know how to play this game, all this makes it a no-buy for me as I mainly play wife my wife, and all these things will turn her off, so it's a "Don't buy" game.

[/b]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A fun and addictive little deckbuilder, despite a few imperfections.

The faction/ally system is interesting even though it does admittedly make the strategies pretty straightforward. It still packs quite a bit of tension into a really short, fast-paced experience.

This is the first deckbuilder I've played where you and your opponent are not offered the same cards for purchase. This raises the luck factor a bit, but is the only way I can see a truly combat-focused deckbuilder work without stalemating and makes for some fun tension (the "oh god I have to buy that so he can't" mentality).

This also isn't really the most balanced game. The yellow faction in particular seems incredibly powerful as there's really no good counter to their discard ability.

Another drawback shared by all these small-box deckbuilders is that the game does lack the kind of replayability that something like Dominion has where you're NOT using every card every game. And because this is a little more straightforward than something like Valley of the Kings... well, you will basically see everything the game has to offer after only a couple of games. Usually I wouldn't recommend a game where that's the case BUT despite that it's actually seen really consistent play for us. Thanks to the short playtime, great in-game pacing, and sheer fun factor this is actually gets pulled out on the "we haven't played this in awhile, why not do a quick game?" basis.

Oh and side note: it sucks that there's not support for more players out of the box, but at this price point it's hard to complain.

Took me awhile to try this with 3 & 4 players and I was surprised by how much I enjoyed it, but we did have to house rule something for 2v2: [b]do not play with shared money[/b]. Shared money only encourages people to build up one person's deck as fast as they can while disregarding their partners' almost entirely. It's way too easy to win this way and it's simply not fun. By retaining shared health, but not currency, this issue is largely fixed.

The only thing that kind of sucks is that bases tend not to stick around in 2v2 for very long, since you're doing massive quantities of damage. Sort of wondering if base values should be upped a little, or something. But again, for a short and not particularly deep game, this doesn't bother me.

Update: Colony Wars is out! Hooray for not having to buy two of the same game just to get to 4p!

I'm not a fan of deckbuilders because I hate counting every single turn as it really takes me out of the game and all I feel like I'm doing is adding stuff every single turn. In that way it feels no different than Dominion as instead of counting coins and actions you are counting trade and attack. As a card game it's probably a 4 because it just feels like math homework to me. 

However, I've found playing this on the app to be great fun as I don't have to worry about the math and I actually feel like I'm building my space fleet and either attacking my opponent or defending myself. As an app it's probably an 8 as it really has the addictive just one more game feel and I wish I felt the same with the physical version.

So I'll average this to a 6 and likely look to part with my physical copy assuming the expansions end up coming to the app as well.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
UPDATE 8/15: In the end, no. I just don't see the edge of deeper decision making that elevates this beyond the category of slightly amusing pastime. It's virtually impossible to devise a meaningful strategic plan at the start of the game (given the information you have and more critically do not have), and there are virtually no interesting decisions to be made from hand to hand.

You play your cards and you take what drops off the conveyor belt and in the end someone wins. It's fine, it's a pleasant distraction when I want to be half-engaged in something, but not a great game, or even a good game, if you're looking to be challenged by difficult strategic questions. You could maybe rig up a pseudo-LCG from this by drafting a subset of the deck and making it your personal trade row, but why not just play a proper LCG? Or if you're not hung up on the deck building thing and you just want a game with a small footprint, sci-fi theme and low price tag, just throw down a few more ducats for Race For The Galaxy.

---

I am still not a fan of deckbuilders that give you a random and ever-changing array of cards in the center of the table, much preferring the Dominion approach of multiple copies of a subset of the game's total card inventory being always available.

Having said that, Star Realms is far more indebted to Ascension, a game with which it has a Marvin Gaye/Robin Thicke level of mechanical similarity, with the critical distinction that in this case, the derivative work is a substantial improvement on the original. Ascension is not a total dumpster fire of a game -- it's an entertaining enough 10 minute iPad distraction -- but between the aforementioned ever-changing row of cards to buy and the removal of Dominion's caps on card purchases and actions, it eliminated a lot of the tactical edge from the granpappy of them all, not to mention intentionally hamstringing the designer's abilities to create cards that have interesting synergies or cards that do things besides giving you points, money or ass-kickin'ness.

Star Realms, for its part, replicates these faults, which does clip its wings as a design. But unlike in Ascension, where the occasional card synergy (based on its curiously underdeveloped four suits/colors/factions/whatever they're called) can be stumbled upon if the whims of the gods of the center row and the whims of your deck align just right, Star Realms, through the addition of the "ally" mechanic, gives you potentially useful synergies with almost any pair of cards of the same color, which is by itself enough to vault it from the ranks of "ugh" to the ranks of "not bad."

In the first rush of exploring the thing, I'm kind of getting into it, truth be told.

The question becomes: will it last? One does not want to draw too many inferences from price point, which is easily one of board gaming's least useful indicators of Quality, but one can't but help wondering if the game's Bic lighter MSRP indicates that the makers of Star Realms are aiming for burning out over fading away. Maybe; or maybe they're just taking a big swing at the mass market. It sure is accessible enough. But for a wizened strategy gamer it may end up being temporarily distracting shiny thing. We shall see.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Star Realms is simply a ton of fun packed into a simple deck building game.  The learning curve is fairly shallow, most players will understand all the important concepts in 5-10 minutes.  

That doesn't mean the game itself is shallow.  Like any good deck building game there are a variety of possible play styles and even a variety of potential solo, co-op, 1v1, team-based, or free for all game setups. I appreciate games that perform well in a variety of formats like that. 

The game might need some work in the balance department.  While in theory all players have access to the same market pool, certain factions are inherently more powerful than others.  The mech faction for instance, is one of the only empires that can scrap (remove) non-useful cards from your deck and purify it into a more efficient fighting force.  For this reason it's almost always in the interest of players to take these cards whenever they see them.  

But the outcome of each game is largely determined by which cards appear in the market and which player is able to afford the best ones first.  I've had a few very fun "close games" but more often than not, there is a clear victor halfway through. 

Still, I will gladly play Star Realms any day of the week

RATING: 6
I vastly prefer the Dominion-style deck-builders (all your options for purchase available from the beginning) over the Ascension-style deck-builders (narrow selection of randomly drawn cards available), but this game pretty much perfects the Ascension style. The best benefit of this style is probably the painless and quick set-up. Sure, I prefer Dominion and its ilk, but is it worth the extra 15 minutes of set-up? In that same timeframe, you could have set up and played a whole game of Star Realms.

RATING: 3
This is an extremely poor game. 
Having played 500 odd games  now (56%) win rate (no idea if thats good or bad or indifferent). The game is 2% strategy, 8% sk.... no i can bring myself to write that, no lets say 6% 'correct card selection', + 2% 'scrap/shuffle manage ability' and 90% total luck. Most of the time you are simply just be along for the ride. The choices in this game a generally so obvious, you'd have to be a .............. (insert own word) not to realise them. If you are playing against an opponent who knows what they are doing you can generally tell 75% of the time by turn 10-12 who will win. Thats one thing in its favour, as you can then simply resign and start a new game. You should be able to get through 15-20 games an hour like that. Sure every now and again you'll be surprised by laughable bad draws, card combo's etc but really what the point. It offers superb illusions of choice etc which really aren't there and youare simply better of playing whist or snap.....

RATING: 6
This feels like a two-player re-theme of Dominion.  The game feels more tense and engaging with only two players.  The level of player interaction is also higher because instead of points each player has hit points (called authority) that the other is trying to cut down.

Is there strategy to this?  A little, but not much.  Two-player Race for the Galaxy is incredibly more strategic.  Nevertheless, for a $15 game that only takes 20 minutes, it's hard to give a lower rating.

RATING: 7
Addicting, to say the least. Its simplicity is its biggest strength. Each faction does different things well and they're unique enough that they have their own personality. The key is to have the balance right - buying only one set of cards is not the best idea, nor is watering down and buying all of them without any aim. 

Unfortunately, I feel like decision-making is a little bit limited in this game. In most cases it's just selecting right cards from the trade row and when it comes to your turn, playing all of them. Thankfully it's satisfying enough that I'm not going to abandon Star Realms anytime soon.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I can't say enough about this game.  It plays quick.  It's interactive.  It has card drafting.  It builds upon itself.  It has great art.  It is small and portable.  It allows for long-term strategy as well as short-term tactics.

Do you thin your deck using the Machine Cult and make a lean and efficient force?  Do you neutralize your opponent's forces before they can even be played with the Star Empire?  Do you maximize your authority with the Trade Federation as you amass your forces?  Or do you file off the safety and go on a rampage with the Blobs?

Has your opponent jumped out to a big lead?  Go heavy on outposts and look for Trade Federation cards to replenish your Authority.

Have a lot of powerful cards, but keep drawing Scouts and Vipers?  Invest in some Machine Cult ships and scrap those useless ships that keep getting in the way.

And always buy the Brain World.  Always.

Dominion lost its appeal long ago, but this game has rekindled my interest in deck builders.  Maybe it will fade over time, but as of now, I have played 27 F2F games, and 4000+ iOS games... and I still can't wait to play more!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
I think this is a great game that's quick to play, and its small size makes it easy to carry on the go. The only thing that keeps me from rating Star Realms a 10 as is: its awkward scoring card system. It really slows the game down. There are great scoring apps out there for Android or Apple, and I recommend downloading and utilizing the app rather than scoring cards right from the beginning. Deckbuilding per jugar 1x1, frenètic. Tipus Neuroshima en el sentit que has d'eliminar els 50 punts de vida (autoritat) del rival.
Hi ha un "Line-up" (o Trade List) de 5 cartes que es van renovant (tipus DC) + 1 mazo ("plates" del Dominion).
Hi ha 4 faccions, cadascuna especialitzada en algo. Lo ideal és anar comprant cartes de 1 o 2 faccions, ja que són realment sinèrgiques entre la mateixa facció: és a dir, hi ha cartes verdes que tenen un +2 atac si hi ha alguna altra carta verda jugada, lo que premia centrar-se en una facció.
També hi ha "bases": cartes que quan les jugues les poses davant teu i sempre et proporcionen algo (i algunes serveixen d'escut de la teva nau base).

This game is very, very similar to Ascension, but with less variety of cards. That's not necessarily bad; just different. It's a little more fragile in terms of unbalanced cards deciding the game, though.

I love the artwork and graphic design. It reminds me of those old Terran Trade Authority SF art books from the 70s and 80s. (Google it if you don't know them -- they were great!)

Want another quick-playing deck-builder? You could do worse than dropping ten bucks on this.

RATING: 6
Eh, a little better than [i]Ascension[/i] I suppose in that there's direct player conflict and no real setup time. I guess it does have a [i]bit[/i] more decision making than [i]Ascension[/i] in that you have to choose your initial strategy (color) but, since the same types of cards feed off of each other, once you've picked it, your choices are largely obvious after the first round or two. Then it's just down to the timing of your hand and flop draws. And it's not like there's much tension, either, since you always play all of your cards. Just not much there, really.

At the end of the day, just filler, though it has found a place on my iPad as a time killer.

Update: I've begrudgingly had to upgrade my rating, which is pretty rare for me. Copious online play has revealed that there's more strategy to this game that I initially wanted to admit. Knowing which cards to buy and when to buy them was not as obvious as I first thought. There's definitely a trick to it, and although you always play your entire hand, sometimes the order in which you play your cards (i.e. deck thinners and outpost abilities) can matter. The draws can still hose you, so there's definitely an element of luck that can occasionally decide the close games, and I definitely think the cost of some of the cards, such as Recycling Station, are off. Still, it's head & shoulders above [i]Ascension[/i] which did NOT reveal anything more interesting with online play.

RATING: 10
Maybe it's because this is my first deck builder but I love this game. The fact that this game is very easy to learn and 1v1 games can take around 20 minutes or even less makes this game quick and enjoyable IMO. Not to mention that it includes rules to play several different game types that support up to 6 players (You need at least 2 boxes of the base game to play with more than 2 people). The only downsides are that this game is lacking in depth and also they give you cards with numbers to keep track of your authority (which is basically your life). I just use a pen and paper. I highly recommend this game if you like deck builders, sci-fi, or quick learning group games.

Multiplayer game types:

Free For All - 3+ players - Pretty obvious how this works.

Hunter - 3+ players - You can only attack players authority to the left of you. You can also attack player bases to the left OR right of you. 

Hydra - Teams of 2v2 or 3v3 with a combined authority. A team all goes at the same time and can combine their trade pool to buy things and combine their combat pools to attack bases or authority. Outposts protect the entire team from damage and must be destroyed first.

Emperor - 3v3 team game where everyone has their individual authorities. Each team has an emperor in the middle and two admirals (one on either side of him). The emperor can attack any enemy while the admirals can only attack the enemy admiral across from them. If an admiral defeats the enemy admiral across from him he may now target the emperor. When an emperor is defeated their team loses the game.

Raid - 3-6 players (1 boss and 2-5 raiders). 1 player is the boss and the rest are raiders. Basically the boss has higher authority and a larger hand size to make up for the lopsided teams. Raiders can discuss strategies but have separate authorities and do not share their trade or combat pools.

Coop Challenges - 1-6 players - There are currently only 2 challenges which are found in the Gambit Set. Up to 6 players can work to defeat either the Nemesis Beast Or Pirates of The Dark Star. Both of these challenges look hard.

RATING: 7
[2016-02-10 Rating: 6 -> 7]
After playing the Star Realms app a lot lately, I feel the need to update my rating. There are quite a lot of things to learn if you want to up your chances of winning: Which are the "key cards" that define your overall strategy? When do I switch from merely building my deck into the full blown attack mode? Which cards do I have to deny my opponent? All this questions popped up with a better understanding and  knowledge of the (base) deck and kept me coming back over and over again. Addicting stuff. 

Currently I think that the most important part of the game is the buying phase, here it is where you make your decision. Unfortunately, the playing of cards from hand doesn't offer that many opportunities for clever play. Sure, sometimes you have to decide if you want to scrap a card for the money or not, but most of the times you just have live what you have been dealt which directly corresponds to your buys. But who knows, maybe I'll discover more depth here with more plays. Until then, Star Realms is solid 7 for me.

[2014-12-08 Rating: n.a. -> 6]

It's just not as deep as the two games I mentioned earlier. But for the price, this one is a steal.

RATING: 8.75
This is basically the same game as Ascension with a different theme.  In fact, the game play is to similar that I think of this as an expansion to Ascension.

With that said, Star Realms has become my favorite version of Ascension.  It's a very well balanced set of cards.  They have great interaction which leads to an interestingly dynamic game.  There is a tremendous amount of skill in the game, which is what keeps it interesting for so many plays.  At the same time, there is a huge amount of luck in regards to whether cards of the same faction are drawn in pairs or not.  It's the balance between these two aspects that keep the game interesting.  Even when you have a very good position, there is usually  an outside chance that an amazing combo of cards can come up to reset things back to parity.

Whether you have or haven't played Ascension, I highly recommend trying Star Realms.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This one seems to have taken the game world by storm.  It's kind of an evolution deckbuilders have been going through, making the transition from strict economic engine euro to a more freeform, combo-y, interactive style reminiscent of a CCG.  Star Realms is the latest step in this progression, and it's quite good fun.  
Plays fast and has lots of choices about which cards to put into your deck, when to trash them out of it, how many factions to dabble with, and so on.  The 5-cards display is kind of limiting, and you can find yourself staring at a bunch of expensive cards you can't afford, with no option but to buy explorers or pass.  Of course, it can be very random.  If you win the race to buy a particularly strong card, you'll be well ahead early.  You can continue to buy suboptimal things, only to flip great stuff for your opponent once you're out of funds.  You can commit to a color, only to have it completely dry up in the display.  There are things that will veto cards out of that display, but not enough, imo, and limited to the blob faction.  Trashing things from your deck is similarly limited to the machine cult.  Asymmetries like that bother me a little, but as long as both players know what they're doing, it's not usually a problem.  Because of this, games can be one-sided blowouts, or they can be tense matches that come down to who draws their good cards on the last couple turns.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Great for the price and how portable it is, but I'd still take dominion or ascension over it as far as deck building games go.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.1
Simple deck building game a la Dominion (read: Ascension) where you are buying powerful space ships and bases in 4 different factions (colors)  and playing them in front of you, possibly making combos with the cards of the same faction, to ultimately destroy you opponent.
It's fast, fun, a bit random and cheap, and it requires additional deck to play with more than 2 players which I have yet to try.

[b]Update:[/b] Rating modified according to a custom rating system.

[b]Favorite mechanism:[/b] 4 conceptually different public factions/races that you can combine from and synergies between space ships of the same color (race) through deckbuilding.

RATING: 5
Head-to-head Ascension describes this one pretty accurately. Reduce your opponent to zero health (authority) to win. I pretty much feel like your best plays are to buy bases always... they provide continual abilities (at least until destroyed) and some can protect you from taking some damage. Maybe I haven't played it enough but that's my opinion. I think I'm in the minority, but I definitely like Ascension more in many respects (artwork, card effects, etc). Ascension also has an advantage since it has so many expansion released at this point.

I'm not one for playing physical sets of deckbuilding games. I was happy to hear of the digital version, but it's just not as slick as the Ascension implementation. It was nice knowing you Star Realms.

Physical version:

-Card stock not great, might need to get sleeves...

-Packaging not going to survive much abuse.

*the base game is free, and EVERYTHING else costs money, so watch out...

RATING: 7
Great filler deck building game. Only really good with two players or teams, since the way that attacks work will not translate well to a free for all. I'm fine with that, its great at two. It feels a bit like a very interactive Ascension. All the typical deck building issues apply. Thinning starting cards, synergy and comboing as well as tempo and ramping all seems fairly well integrated. There is luck of the draw but it still feels largely like you have some control on the games outcome. The single player senerios are very draw dependent though, and will showcase how much luck of the draw can play in this game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
A nice introductory deck builder, but it doesn't stack up to its competition. I really like the ideas in place, especially the chaining of powers between faction cards, but this same mechanism makes it incredibly difficult to pivot strategies and play adaptively. The early game decisions pigeonhole players for the rest of the game, leaving them to rely solely on the market to pan out for them. Granted the game is short, so this lack of flexibility can be forgiven. Keeping track of your authority is dreadfully tedious, though.

[b]previous comment:[/b]

(played mostly on the phone or tablet apps, although I own the physical version too)

Star Realms is a game that borrows a lot from earlier deck builders, and I am a little bit surprised at its popularity.  It suffers from the same problem as the Cryptozoic deck builders, whose "path" system is very similar - too much randomness.  Not only can you get screwed in the randomness by not drawing the right card combinations to trigger faction-specific effects, but you can get screwed by the randomness by choosing to buy a crappy card, because it's your only option, only to have the card you actually wanted replace it, and get bought by your opponent.  In fact, I'd argue that Star Realms is MORE random than the Cryptozoic deck builder.

I originally rated it slightly higher than my favorite Cryptozoic game, Lord of the Rings DBG, for one reason:  The combat system.  This direct conflict is a nice twist compared to most minimally interactive deck builders, and definitely adds to the game.  Granted, it's extremely swingy, tends to snowball, and the card-based influence counters are a pain to use, but it's still fun to pull off a 30-point combo and beat the crud out of your opponent.

After further play, I realized that the only reason I rated Star Realms higher than LOTR or DC was that I was playing on the app, which was making the game super-fast and hiding the negative impact of the extra susceptibility to randomness.

For $15, this game is a decent value, but it certainly wasn't worth the hype levels where people were paying twice MSRP to buy it when stores started running out of stock.  The APP VERSION of this game should also completely fire any of the plethora of Cryptozoic deck builders for most people, since it does what they do in a quicker game with the extra fun of combat.  On the table, it's a crap shoot - the combat is more fun but the game is more dominated by randomness.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
Star Realms is one of the myriad Dominion look-alikes. It uses a shared center row (see Ascension) instead of a fixed card pool and focuses on immediate confrontation rather than Dominion's more sedate (and sedating) combo-building, giving it a rather different feel in play. The available factions feature distinctly different abilities and the game actually encourages faction specialization which is nice.

Card effects are all fairly simple and a game is over quickly so the luck of the shared center row isn't overbearing. Despite the conflict-driven gameplay the game actually feels more like a "comfort zone" game if that makes any sense. It flows quite pleasantly, you can start playing right away and without giving it too much of a thought but I'm wondering whether it has enough substance to sustain repeated plays over a longer period of time. Only time will tell, I suppose.

RATING: 6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Counter-play seems to exist between most factions: the Blob want big hands, so they hate the Star Empire; the Star Empire struggles to put out a large enough force to be able to knock down the Trade Federation's outposts and authority as fast as they are put up; and the Trade Federation cannot handle the Blob's massive brute force. The Machine Cult however is an utterly game deciding faction due to the pure strength of their scrapping which doesn't even hold an opportunity cost. If one player is unlucky enough to never have a Machine Cult card in the Trade Row on their turn, they have practically no chance of winning.
The lack of constraints in playing cards makes the deck building trivially easy much like the other well known deck builder from this designer. The Allied Abilities (that trigger with other cards of similar factions) are the biggest consideration that goes into buying cards-- though often a player does not want to buy cards simply because a better card might appear for their opponent!
The power ramp is huge, fast, and erratic. It is a reasonably fun ride and the game ends gracefully the moment a player begins to pull ahead rather than dragging on when the winner is certain.

(16 plays)
First Impressions: 6

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Star Realms is (in my opinion) a more interesting version of Ascension. Mechanics for adding to your deck are streamlined and the addition of regular abilities to thin out your deck are much appreciated as well. Where I have issues with this game is the overall snowballing of the winner that I have experienced; a player who is able to purchase a powerful card and then get lucky enough to be able to use it on the following turn just seems to get so much of an advantage (particularly so early on in the game) that it's hard to come back from. I don't think this is necessarily a problem, as that's the nature of a game featuring large amounts of randomness, however it doesn't often feel good to be effectively helpless in a game.

--
April 15th, 2015 - Given away to my friend John Ross.

RATING: 7.5
I used to suck big-time at this game and lose almost all matches, until I read some strategy guides. This one is excellent: https://megahaulin.wordpress.com/2015/08/27/the-pace-of-the-game-part-1-a-game-of-four-and-a-half-decks/. Basically hands 1-2 are for buying cards that give money and scrap ability, hands 3-4 for solidifying the chosen strategy (deck colour), and hands after that on locking into this strategy and buying powerful cards. Scrapping (red's ability) is extremely strong so all my decks contain some red cards, but which colour I play depends on which cards I get from the market in the first 1-3 hands.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Quick play deck building lite war game - most like Ascension in it's mechanics but with a good direct conflict system. Because it uses the "centre row of available cards" mechanic the luck of the draw factor is very high. Becomes a bit add-up-ee once you get some ally ships in play and need to count up all those ally activated factors - maybe the app is a better option in that respect.

We have found the strategy a bit limited - go for the big money early and then big combat factors - planets seem a bit underpowered - seems that a defensive strategy is not really viable. 

Card art is good and the card stock is solid, rules are well written and set out and the cards are easy to understand. A small grip is the life point counter system - cards in various denominations that you flip as your life points go up and down - a bit cumbersome - we use beads. Ok.
  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Portable, which I appreciate, but it lacks the production polish that Ascension has. It's hard not to compare this with Ascension and there two key things to keep in mind when comparing the two. In Ascension, you race to defeat monsters that pop up, or you purchase cards with a good amount of victory points, namely Constructs. You and your opponent deplete a pool of points, then it is all tallied at the end. In Star Realms, it's all about attack and defend. You have your cards that allow you purchase beefier ships, than you have you bases to defend and give bonuses, along with your fleet of attack ships. It's far more aggressive and feels more like Mtg, actually. If you are into confrontation, Star Realms is it. Love the artwork.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Smooth-playing deck builder with little scope for interesting maneuvering. The tiny selection of cards on offer + the need to specialize in terms of factions = a very restricted and formulaic decision space.

The faction system doesn't work well because of the dynamics of the two-player draft. If you focus exclusively on your favored one or two factions, then you're letting your opponent do the exact same thing, and the game will simply come down to whose factions appear in the draft queue. If you play more of a mixed value/denial strategy, then neither player will have reliable combos, and the game will come down to whose deck randomly clumps same-faction cards together. Either way, the overall feeling is that my choices don't really matter.

RATING: 6
Competent deckbuilder with scifi art. Plays very similarly to Ascension, but instead of attacking the monsters on the table you attack other players. 

After you picked which way your deck will go the decisions on how to build it are very limited. You get the most expensive card from your faction you can afford.

Has several play modes and can be expanded to add more players.

Most of the illustrations show a ship with some nebula/planet behind it, and they end up looking too similar to each other. 

Kudos for having a free app with asynchronous online play. I just wish the font was bigger!

RATING: 8.5
Wow. Compact, intuitive, and strategic. I started playing this game and didn't even check the clock again until 1am. The different factions feel relatively balanced, but Machine Cult and the necessity of deck-thinning mean you're going to get a bit of red in every good deck. Game length and complexity feel perfect. Drafting options feel streamlined and the gameplay is unique enough to warrant extended sessions. 

Explorers don't feel useful to buy very often, if ever. There is some minimal degree of homogeneity to how games play out, at least once you've settled into an archetype but this is often in the closing few turns anyways.

[b]On Colony Wars:[/b]
Like a tuned-up version of Star Realms, overall power level seems higher but there's also a more uniform level of balance. Don't know that this'll be replacing Star Realms but likely I'll alternate in order to keep the cards fresh.

Have Colony Wars (5 Plays)
Have Promo Pack One (but it's shuffled into Colony Wars)

RATING: 7
It's a solid deck builder, where skill will override luck, most of the time.  However, with the deck being randomized it begins to break down somewhat in that certain ships will only ever be available to one player.  My other concern with the game was that the scale/theme of the game was really lacking.  Federation fighting Federation?  How long of a time frame is one turn?  These things aren't terribly important, but add flare and fun.

The game is a very fun 2-player deck-builder, but lacks any flare to push it higher.

RATING: 8
Wow, just wow. I love Ascension but they really made it better and more streamlined with this. RATING: 8
After 75+ games...

For the price, this is a good game.

It may appear light at first, but there are myriad combos and multiple paths to victory.

Unfortunately, it being a card/deck building game, there is still a sizable luck factor.  Will you have the resources to buy those big cards, or will the big cards be available when you have the resources?  Will the combos come out together, or in different hands?

Team play looks promising, but requires the purchase of an extra game per two players.

If you like light-medium and fast playing deck building games, it would be a good addition to your collection, as a game can be completed in 20-30 minutes, and it has a relatively small foot print.

If you despise randomness, there may be just a bit too much here you.

But you may try before you buy, as the game is available as a free download for most platforms!  So you can learn how to play versus the (easy) computer AI.

RATING: 7
It's comparable to Ascension, but at first blush this seems to be a better game. It's a deck builder with a common pool of randomly replaced cards, like Ascension. The random card appearance seems less annoying to me here, probably because it's only for 2 players... There are four different factions of cards, and specializing in a faction is highly rewarded, as playing more than one card of a faction results in increased payoff. I don't know how balanced it is, but yellow and red factions seem extremely powerful.

Easy to pick up, fast moving, and over before you know it. Overall, I think this is pretty solid.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Good God but the box is some kind of joke—it actually tore as I opened it, and this was going in forewarned of its flimsiness!  I suppose the designers, as [gameid=463] fiends, consider deck packaging to be the equivalent of sausage casings, only there to keep the meaty bits in one place until wolfed down.  I recommend immediate replacement with, say, a translucent deck box.

First couple plays:  Just like the last breath torn from your lungs during a decompression event, this is short and far sweeter than you'd think.  I am especially impressed with what must have been a great deal of editing on the part of the designers in order to arrive at such simplicity when it would have been so easy—and surely seductive—to just keep piling on more and more...

And it turns out I'm a sucker for the badass sci-fi art—any one of those cards would look just plain [i]awesome[/i] airbrushed onto a panel van.

PS.  The Authority Cards (used to track "hit points" for game end) work just fine—they're not nearly as fiddly or inconvenient as others might have you believe.  I love the fact that they allow for the entire game to fit in a standard hobby deck box with no need for extra bits like glass beads, dice, or even paper & pencil.

I dig the self-contained portability of the thing—in the end it's just a deck of cards.  And that's perfect.

Included select cards from:

[thing=166707]Crisis – Events[/thing]
[thing=166708]Crisis – Heroes[/thing]

PURGED

RATING: 7.5
It took me a while before I realized I was not a deck-builder fan. Marvel Legendary sold me on the concept but in hindsight there was a lot of newness and shiny theme going on. The level of self expression in a deck-builder is high and I can see the appeal in that. Star Realms accomplishes all the things I love in a deck builder (combos, planning, and deck customization) with a minimal amount of the things I dislike (setup, constant shuffling, predictability). The expansions did nothing for me.  Side note: The art was a surprisingly miss for me. While I love starships and space imagery, the work here, while fundamentally good, lacks a lot of style and soul. It's almost too colorful and quickly approaches self-published sci-fi novel on Amazon. I know it's tough to design 4 unique factions that inform their playstyle, but the art really holds this game back from a 9.

+Variety of gameplay modes

RATING: 9
A single game is fun. A dozen games show your skill.

I love the theme and the great artwork. It's a lot of fun playing this game. You win some, lose some. But after a while you get the feeling, that winning depends more on luck than on skill. What makes this game seam so totally unbalanced is, that the choice for each player is so completely different.
In Dominion everybody has the same choice and the skill is finding the best mechanic to win. While changing the trade row is more interesting, it makes the game completely random. Machi Koro has a better aproach, since there are less different cards available more often and more cards in "trade row", which makes them stay longer.
Despite all that, skill is very important. You can't just look at it one game at a time. A single game is fun. A dozen games show your skill.

RATING: 9
Currently my favorite 2 player game.  I understand that Star Realms is highly derivative of Ascension, but I am not an Ascension fan.  I love the science fiction theme of Star Realms and the art on the cards.  This game just works very well:  a good deck-builder that tasks players with depleting their opponent's 50 "authority" points..  The base game is very re-playable and fun and a TREMENDOUS gaming value @ $14.00, fits in a small box, very portable.  An excellent game.  If you like deck builders or are looking for a great 2 player game, give Star Realms a try.  I will also mention that it is easy to teach and learn.  You can enjoy Star Realms with non-gamers.

** Update:  I am enjoying Star Realms very much, but sometimes I feel like there is too much randomness.  So it is random--like in all card games--which sequence your cards are drawn into your hand from your draw pile, which is fine.  There are games that are won or lost because my cards were drawn in a fortuitous sequence (or not!) That randomness is compounded with the randomness of the trade offering.  It often seems like I don't have good choices on my turn and after I choose between the turd sandwich or the giant douche, a terrific card replaces the card I took in the offering providing a great benefit for my opponent.  Some games that happens more than once and it's annoying.  I still love the game overall.  It is great for playing with non- or nascent gamers.  It has fun choices, great theming, its a great light game.  It is not a perfect game.  The randomness is a minor flaw.

RATING: 5
A similar style game to Dominion, but also one that I think doesn't quite match up in playability or fun.  First of all, I want to applaud the artwork as it's absolutely brilliant and it really evokes the space exploration and combat theme beautifully.  Unfortunately, I wasn't a big fan of the gameplay, firstly I felt that attacking the other players could be a bit too political (wasn't in my case but it has the potential for other people to be that way), and although you can play it so you only attack to one side this then means that a strong player with a weak player to his right will win every time.  I also think that the bases mechanic was weird as often you buy them only to have them destroyed before you can use them.

It's nice that it plays quickly, and the problems I have with it are therefore bearable but I don't think I would seek out a game of this.

RATING: 6
Played a partial game, seems choices are pretty limited and obvious but I need to play it further, you do see a little of the MTG influence but its much more like Dominion/Ascension (except Dominion the market is constant, here it changes which adds luck element)

Edit - Played about 8 games now, its a neat little mix of Dominion and Magic set in space, easy to learn, fun, great little price tag, plays in a fast amount of time, lots of positives here, really a great little filler game. 

My rating would be higher but what I said above stands for me, decisions are mostly limited and obvious, and of course luck plays a fairly big role (luck is somewhat forgivable given length of game). Sometimes you get a nice choice between cards, but other than this you just play out what is in front of you. Have only played 2 player, no interest in more than 2, feels like there is nothing more to explore after 3 - 5 games much like just about every pure DBG (and much unlike something like Mageknight, A Few Acres, etc)

RATING: 10
I really hoped that I never used this cliche, but here goes: "I'm giving this game a 10 because I can't give it anything higher." God, I hate that cliche. But you know what: Totally appropriate in this case. Star Realms has everything I want in a game: Space/sci-fi theme, great artwork, quick and easy game play, pretty quick play time and (with the addition of the Gambit pack) the ability to play solo. It's also pretty inexpensive, and very portable - I fit the game plus an expansion pack (all sleeved) in a double-wide deck box.

Absolutely brilliant!


EDIT (2 Mar 2015): I didn't anticipate how addictive this game it. My whole family is hooked on it! We've also been playing the app version and I've picked up three expansions. Have also since picked up Ascension as well because I love the gameplay mechanics!

RATING: 10
It is extremely rare I give any game a 9 or a 10. This is definitely one of them. Great mix of CCG and card play, without all of the garbage baggage that comes along with CCG's. Quick to play with just enough options. Plenty of tension and some tough decisions. Base game can be harsh if you have money to spend but low cost cards keep turning up on your turn. But that's part of the fun of the game.

The Gambit cards help balance things by doing creative combos to get you back in the game or pull ahead. So if you do get jammed up with weaker cards, there's usually an out with the gambit cards. You just have to spot it and take advantage of it. You can make some nifty moves with the Gambit cards - the game rewards outside the box thinking.

Still, base game or with expansions, this is great bang for the buck value and is portable.

RATING: 5
2014-09-08

Initial Rating: 5.0 (September 2014)

I bought the game blindly, so it was a surprise when I found out it was an Ascension clone and from one of the designers from Ascension. It is basically the same thing, the currencies are called differently and now you have to kill your opponent, but it has the same core (two currencies, constructs/bases, factions).

I have played it twice, and it seems (so far) that the combat cards are more powerful than the rest. Love the space theme, but I don’t feel it very much. I really dislike the variable market offering. For me that takes away all the strategy from the game and replace it with lots of uncontrollable luck (pure tactics). I was able to win my first game by a lot using this tactic. I always bought the most expensive card I could afford, I didn’t even read the card and presto…. I won. In this kind of games, the luckiest payer will win, that is, the player who has the better cards in the market when is his turn will win. OBVIOUS decisions.

For me, it is ONLY a two player game, with more, the luck factor increases a lot, also player elimination is a huge disadvantage in the multiplayer game. The score system is a bit clunky and it is prone to cause mistakes/cheats, however it allows for the game to be cheap.

OK art, cards on the flimsy side. It can play fast (its main strength), otherwise the rating would be way lower.

Bottom line, I don’t like this kind of game, but for now I will keep my copy as it was very cheap. Also I prefer to play it in the iPad, there the games are much quicker so I don’t mind the uncontrollable luck. Had I played it only on the iPad, the rating would be a bit higher (as is Ascension). Miles away from Dominion. 

Current Rating: 5.0

It has factions, unite abilities, 2 types of currency (attack and money). The twists are in the fact that instead of monsters, you are attacking each other, and when you buy a card, you immediately play it (instead of it going straight into your discard). It's an interesting twist, and I quite like it.

RATING: 7.5
Fast, portable and intuitive deck building game in the tradition of [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/36218/dominion]Dominion[/url] or [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/69789/ascension-chronicle-of-the-godslayer]Ascension[/url], but with its own twist: attacking each other. RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Takes the basic model for Ascension (direct player conflict, revolving cards for purchase) and streamlines it down into a tight little package. The price is certainly right and you do get your money's worth. However, this game will get old for me very quickly. I can tolerate the inherent randomness of the deckbuilding mechanic, but why would I want to increase that randomness with a constantly evolving constellation of cards to purchase? Now do I not only need to draw the right cards, the right cards also need to be available for purchase. This was my main beef with Ascension and it is the same beef here. However, this is much cheaper, smaller and plays a lot quicker. For that, its weaknesses are easier to forgive.

This game has gotten surprisingly popular surprisingly fast and I have to think that is because of the app version, which is well-implemented. In fact, I think this game probably works better as an app. The constant addition and subtraction of points gets a bit tedious in actual card play.

UPDATE: So a few years later I am still playing this with my son (it is his game, not mine) and we always enjoy it when we do. For the longevity that I did not think was there, I am raising my score on this to an 8. I appreciate that this game has gotten my son more into gaming than anything else he has played.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
REVISED 01/2016
After many many playthroughs, both with the cards and on the app version, I have come to realize that this game is far too luck-heavy for me. Yes, the cards are lovely, and yes it is fun, but early on I thought this was a game of deeper strategy. I cannot tell you how many games I have lost because of what was available in the trade row on my turns versus my opponent's turns. I knew all the right moves, but just couldn't make them. Frustrating IF you think you are playing a game rich in strategy. I have come to see that it is just a light filler game with a little longer play time than most fillers. Still like it, but just do not play it nearly as much anymore. END REVISION

The only deck builder I will ever own. I tried Dominion and Ascension first, and they didn't do it for me. Dominion was like playing a game of solitaire, but then scoring it competitively, at the end and both games seemed to suffer from too many expansions. How many f***ing cards does one game really need? My brother-in-law owns Dominion and a whole mess of expansion cards. The setup was tedious to say the least. I watched him selecting this stack, and then that stack, and then second guess and switch for another one. Too much going on for this type of game, in my opinion.

Star Realms is one shared deck that all players purchase from. Simple setup, AND even better: it's interactive, which solves my solitaire beef with Dominion. And at the time of this writing the game is still young, but so far, there have only been a few mini-expansions that add a handful of ships and bases, and a couple new minor mechanics.

A note to the developers: in future, I will no longer be interested in adding more cards to an already large trade deck. Diluting it further lowers the chances of any given card coming out in the trade row during any given game which decreases any ability to plan and strategize. If you want to continue getting my money - and the money of several others who have contributed to threads here on BGG about this excellent game - you should release an expansion that is a fully-realized new faction. The game would still play only 4 factions at a time, but you choose which 4 from the pool.

Even just one new faction drastically improves replay value. Release a few, and you'll keep us coming back for more. You get more of our money per purchase and we get a game that continues to surprise us with a trade deck that does not continue to inflate to cumbersome proportions. While I realize that this would make setup take longer, like I complained about Dominion and its expansions, but this will still not compare. Rifling through one deck and removing say all the blue cards and shuffling in a new color will be quicker than all the hemming and hawing and judgement calls needed to weave together all the different choices in a complete Dominion set.

----------
I love how much game they managed to squeeze into a stack of cards with a $12 price tag! I'm not a huge fan of deck builders but I think this game might overcome my predisposition. It's very streamlined yet retains the flow and interesting decisions of the genre. It's quite fast and if you're willing to try out the multi-player modes there seems to be a lot of flexibility to the game play. I'm particularly interested in the co-op/solo modes which require promo cards which have not been released to retail channels yet. 

The 2p game is fast! Your deck ramps up quickly and before you know it you can blast people for 20+ damage on a turn. The four factions have well defined mechanical differences and it's interesting to play around with combos. 

Easily worth the price of admission, although we'll have to wait and see if it has any long term appeal.

RATING: 7
Star Realms distillates the essence of deckbuilding into a small package. The combos are color-coded into four factions, making use of Star Realms as an introductory deckbuilder feasible. It's developed for two players although it has various multiplayer rules that appear to be more than an afterthought.
As most players, I got to know this via the app for this game. I'm not too fond of deckbuilding games but Star Realms works for me. It doesn't have the never-ending combos I dread so much in Dominion, the deck-thinning prerogative is only one of the four faction-induced focal strategies. The luck factor is higher than in most other games of this type but it's short enough that I'm fine with that.
