RATING: 10
Counting online, this is by far my most played game.  An incredible design, and the fact that it's contained in a box barely larger than a deck of standard playing cards and retails for $15 is just gravy.  The portability means you can take it anywhere, it's fast and easy to set up, and typically plays in 15-20 minutes.  Sounds like the perfect filler, right?  And yet it is so much more.  Do not be fooled by the high variance/luck factor, there is a lot of skill needed to do well at this game.  It is more like poker than chess, where optimal play will not always win, but over the long haul you will win more than you lose.

As compared to Dominion, it is more tactical and less strategic since there is a variable "trade row" of cards you can buy and add to your deck (as in Ascension which I have not played).  This adds more luck/variance to the equation, but the upside is tremendous replayability as no two games are ever alike.  It is also more thematic with much better artwork (not that that's too high a bar lol).  Also, even though a number of great expansions have been released, they are not necessary to enjoy the game for a long time, unlike base Dominion which got stale after a shorter period.

About the only downside is that scoring and keeping track of different card combos in play can be a little tedious when playing face to face.  The game comes with double-sided score cards in different denominations (1/5, and 10/20) that you flip over to keep track of your score.  Some people hate these, but I for one appreciate that the designers included a card-based scorekeeping mechanism that could fit into the small tuckbox in which the game comes packaged.  

I will also mention there is a great online community of players, including a BGG Star Realms league, and one-off tournaments run through the fan-created facebook page.  

approximate number of plays (pre-2017):  100+

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Fantastic game. Really well done, and surprising in its depth and addictiveness!

I picked this up at the FLGS on impulse. Last night, my regular Dominion group was scheduled to come over, but all but one canceled, so I introduced this to him (he is also a regular Star Wars LCG opponent of mine). He loved it more than I dared hope, and we played four rapid fire games. He was so impressed (as was I) that I went out today and bought a second copy.

I loved the gameplay. The four factions are distinct, and there is often an unhappy choice as to what to buy - you often want more than one option, but the ally ability is so key, that it really encourages you to streamline your deck!

I want more cards and options already, and am looking forward to some 3- and 4-player games soon.

The cards themselves are really great. I ripple shuffled them and they had a good snap to them, but side-shuffling left some minor nicks on them, so I ended up sleeving them in my normal KMC Clears. Now the stack of cards is high enough that adding a second 80-card stack is going to require some special accoutrements.

The life counting is a little fiddly - I forsook the enclosed cards, really wishing they had instead printed other usable cards on them, and used pen-and-paper (i played all 4 games with my pencil over my right ear...). This is really the only reason I gave the game a 9.5. The art is great but not jaw-dropping, and the names of the cards are a little silly (the Battle Mech is a ship...and one race are the "Blobs"...) but the mechanics and play are really enjoyable. I highly recommend this and am looking forward to other expansions!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A fast paced card game that is easy to learn and fun to play. The rules are simple and decisions are straightforward but there is more depth than it first appears. This combined with a quick play time makes this one a winner that keeps you coming back for more.

It is a deck builder where you build your deck as you play. Each turn you are buying cards to place into your own deck and, while you are sure to have favorites, the value of every card really depends on the current game state (what else is in your deck? what else is in your opponents deck? what else is available to buy? how close are we to the end? etc). Evaluating this is what keeps the game fresh as, though it usually seems like a pretty easy decision what to buy, it is actually hard to be 100% certain that is the best decision.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
Goofy deck building game, with lots of direct interaction. At its core, it's very similar to Ascension, a game from the same designers. Thankfully, this one is way more polished and balanced.

While the deck building component is interesting, the way the cards are drafted makes mitigating the luck factor difficult. While all card games have an inherit luck component, in Star Realms it's higher than average. Even though experienced players will consistently trump newbies, in terms of equal skill between players, games are decided by sheer luck more often than I would've liked.

But considering the game is both short and has a high take-that component, I don't find the aforementioned luck factor to be that much of an issue. The flow of play is fun and engaging when draws are balanced and, when they're not, the game ends swiftly enough to not become frustrating.

All in all, fun but not great. If I had to draw comparisons with other games, I'd say it's Mag Blast meets Dominion: it's got the goofiness and direct confrontation of the first, mixed with the deck building component of the latter. It's also easy to teach, which is always a plus. 

P.S.: I'm very intrigued by the expansion which introduces a co-op mode.

RATING: 1.5
This game is 99% luck, still 1% better than Hearthstone, but that's not saying a lot. You will get screwed over by this game a lot. Prepare to do nothing wrong and gets slaughtered 50+ to -10+ based on nothing but "You get a good card but reveal a great card your opponent gets on their turn." Or, the always wonderful "You never combo even with 5 of the same faction in your deck but your opponent manages to combo every single time." Or try the "They always just happen to get the precise Trade to get the great card in the offer the turn before you can buy it." Or the great one "You are always 1 Trade short of buying any of the good cards no matter how many good trade-based cards are in your deck."

You will rage quit this game so many times over these, and I didn't even mention that the game can easily be called on Turn 1 if the Freighter is in the offer because whoever gets that card will win 99% of the time. Don't buy this game, and don't buy the App/PC version. It's bad for your health.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Star Realms is a fast paced deck-building card game of outer space combat. It combines the fun of a deck-building game with the interactivity of Trading Card Game style combat. As you play, you make use of Trade to acquire new Ships and Bases from the cards being turned face up in the Trade Row from the Trade Deck. You use the Ships and Bases you acquire to either generate more Trade or to generate Combat to attack your opponent and their bases. When you reduce your opponent’s score (called Authority) to zero, you win!

RATING: 7
I only played the app so far. I still find the game interesting, it does not surpass Rune Age which is best from my point of view.

I dislike the common pool of cards for drafting but at 2 players it does not change much and most cards have no text, so its half a problem. I still prefer private pools like in Rune Age.

I like cards that can self destruct themselves for additional abilities. They encourage focusing drafting  same faction, but you might not survive without the help of other factions. Since all cards have synergies with other cards of the same color, it makes balancing easier.

After reading other people's comment, it could be true that it plays better as an app since it's quick and will mitigate bad luck when playing single player. There are some cards more powerful than others for the same cost, but in general the game is roughly balanced.

The app offer the Challenge mode which seems more strategic than the base game. So it's possible that the real card game becomes boring more quickly.

Final important note, this game is one of the few deck building card games that has the possibility to attack or interact directly with your opponent. This is normally something I search in deck building game, Rune Age being the first game that did this, but few designer seems interested in doing that. Just because of that, I'll give a +1 to the game's rating.

RATING: 7.8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Star Realms is a deckbuilding game set in a sci-fi universe featuring four warring star fleets. As in any deckbuilding game you're playing cards in order to acquire other cards that offer you better playing options. The four different factions offer interesting combinations with powerful effects when played together. The objective of the game is to reduce your opponents authority (hitpoints) from 50 to zero.

I wasn't that impressed with Star Realms when I first played it.  But after multiple plays during which I've come to learn the cards and faction powers, I started enjoying the game more and more. I love the theme. And even though my wife doesn't like the theme, she does like the game and is very good at it. For that reason, it'll probably stay in our collection for a long time.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
[u]Awards:[/u]
[b]Game of the Con - GenCon 2014[/b] (Shared with [gameid=143884])
[b]Special Award for Gaming Awesomeness - 2014[/b]

[u]Comments:[/u]
Star Realms shocked me, like how seeing your ex with riding a bull would probably shock you. I thought I was done with deckbuilder games after my love of Thunderstone faded and I just felt deckbuilder fatigue. Every game seemed to be the same abstracted slog that really wasn't scratching any itches that I had. I, like many of you, had heard about this new deckbuilder that was only $15 and extremely hard to get your hands on. So when I went ot GenCon 2014 and saw they had a good number of tables running demos I decided to give it a shot, and I am so glad I did. This game is what every deckbuilder wishes it could be. It has direct player interaction, and extremely streamlined gameplay. With four factions, and numerous different types of ships the synnergy strategies are nearly endless. When I first got the game I thought sticking to 2 factions was going to be the primary winning strategy for this game, but I have seen so many other strong strategies that I know that the depth of gameplay is there. Many are saying Star Realms is similar to Ascension and that is a fair assesment, but it is also a lot more fun than Ascension. With the introduction to semi-permanent cards (Bases), synergy bonuses (Faction Bonuses), and trashing abilities the game is culmination of five years of deckbuilding history, and while it stands on the shoulders of it's predecessors it also reaches heights that are unheard of for any other deck builder. After playing my demo game I showed it to all four of the people in my group at GenCon and 3 of them bought the game. The price point combined with the streamlined and easy game play is absolute winner. When you can, do yourself a favor and buy Star Realms.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Really a great game all-around.  The fastest deckbuilder and also the simplest to teach.  The strategy is also a lot deeper than you'd initially think; I'm a long-time deckbuilder player and even after playing this 100 times I realize I am not a master as I don't win every game.  The only drawback is that a fair amount of luck is involved, but that's to be expected for being a LIGHT game.  

At the same time, the main selling point is it is SO easy to learn; just four factions and two types of cards.  There are more types of cards in the Crisis expansions, which are GREAT btw; simple but add a couple more elements with hero cards that you use once at an opportune moment and event cards that effect everyone immediately when they come up.  

Lots of replayability, good light strategy that's deeper than it looks, great art, very elegant game, and amazing price point to boot.  Although I would recommend sleeving your cards and buying the $20 long deckbox, you still come out ahead of a normal deckbuilder game.  Truly all you could want in a game.

UPDATE: the one thing I don't like is that there is a lot more randomness to winning than I thought.  It feels like only 20% skill, 80% randomness, whereas I thought it was more the other way around at first.  This is a filler game, of course.  The games really do only take 15 minutes.  And I use this on people who don't play games, so it makes them feel good to actually win sometimes.  So it's a drawback but not a dealbreaker.

RATING: 6

At least the quick bursting down of your opponents mean since you don't use that many cards each game you can get a lot of plays before the game becomes stale.

EDIT 2 after playing over 200 times vs. the app AI: Still extremely addicting, but god is this game swingy. Based on what cards show up after you draft them from the central row your opponents might get insane color combos, or just luck out with a couple cards they have left, or getting your +3 money outpost at the start of your card draw instead of at the end, any of these factors can basically mean game over for the opponent. Completely unacceptable if this game was longer.

RATING: 8
I'm told this plays similarly to Ascension, though I don't have a first-hand basis for that.

Star Realms is a fairly simple deckbuilder whose primary virtue is its ease of portability / setup / takedown. A starter deck for each player, a single always-available purchase deck roughly analogous to Silver in Dominion, and a single shuffle-everything deck for purchase.

Two players compete head-to-head over the cards currently available for purchase, and so the game is substantially more tactical than strategic. The purchasing system can be undeniably swingy (for instance, if one better-than-the-rest income card is up for grabs early). For a short light game that lends itself to multiple plays in a row, I think that's fine.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Very fun game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Type: directly competitive deckbuilder

Best with: 2 players
Weight: Light
Duration: 5-25 minutes
Notes:
direct damage competitive
not much strategy, mostly tactical
luck based on card draw from built decks as the game goes on

Star Realms is a remarkably approachable and portable deck-building card game. Both players start with a basic deck of cards. They purchase additional cards from the tableau in the middle, and then attack the other player with attack cards until only one is left standing.

On the upside, this game is about as basic and simple as a deckbuilder game will get. It is head-to-head, which is a nice thing to have, since many deckbuilders are coop or more like multiplayer solitaire in nature. Despite the simplicity, the sheer number of cards available in the base deck mean this game always looks different each time you play it, and there can be some strategic choices you need to make when looking at card synergy and the balance between defense and offense.

On the downside, there are not many tricks to be had. You just need to attempt to find some synergy in the cards you purchase, ramp your deck up smoothly, and hope luck is on your side. Beyond that, the game strips the deckbuilder head-to-head genre down to its raw basics.

Also, not all players will appreciate the bash-each-other 2 player game, but the game is quick and wins can go back and forth pretty quickly.

Lastly, it must be mentioned that the score-tracking mechanism is based on actual cards with number values on them which you add to or subtract as damage is dealt. While I sympathize with the intent to make the game as portable as possible, this score-tracking is terrible and should be replaced with a few 2d10 dice for each player. Even a rotating counter that is card-sized would be a better option.

Sometimes, this game is a bit too light for my tastes. Maybe I want something a little deeper like a Thunderstone match, or a few rounds of Lord of the Rings, or even Pixel Tactics or Magic to get my card game fix. At other times, though, this game is just right for some light, quick card action.

Overall, this game is excellent to just have in a back pocket and whip out while on a train, in a waiting room, or at a con with a buddy or even strangers. Easy game to set up and teach, as well. Merges somewhat seamlessly into a 3-4 player game, even if you don't cart along all of the purchasable cards each time.

Similar games in my collection: Magic: The Gathering (1-on-1 customizable card game), Puzzle Strike (multiplayer competitive deckbuilder), Legendary (coop deckbuilder), Thunderstone Advance (coop deckbuilder),

RATING: 3.5
I've wondered about this game for a while. The art and theme are so appealing. I originally put off buying it because I wanted to buy the expansions too, and one of them has been out of print for a while. I decided to try the digital version on my phone. The mechanic of this game is flawed. While deck building games usually make you acquire V.P., this one makes use of life points (authority) instead. So basically, you have to chip away at your opponent's H.P. before he/she get yours down to zero. If they managed to fix that, then this would be a great game, but as it is, the game is too short which make developing a strong strategy (or any strategy for a matter of fact) impossible. I'm glad I didn't waste my money on this one. If you want a good deck builder, go for Ascension. I'll say this at least for Star Realms, it's better than Dominion. But then again, any DB is.

RATING: 8
At first glance, this may seem to be just a simplified rerig of Ascension, but the changes make for a very different game. And a better one, if compared to only the Core Set of Ascension. Apart from excellent faction differentiation, I especially like the use of bases for both defense and some additional power during a turn. With very little rules overhead, they add interesting decisions when attacking your opponent.

I haven’t tried any of the multiplayer variants yet, but the two-player duel is short, tense and addictive as hell. And the card illustrations are excellent, even if they’re not as unique as the quirky art in Ascension.

RATING: 7
A fun little card game that's easy to learn and easy to play, but with very little depth to it. Your only real strategic decision is in which cards you purchase, and even then, there's no guarantee you'll get those cards when you need them. Someone can be chipping away at their opponent's health, only to have their opponent play something crazy like 20+ damage in a single turn due to nothing else but luck of the draw.

And while everyone has the opportunity to have crazy draws like this, it usually ends up (at least when I've played it) that one person completely obliterates the other, with their opponent having no real way to retaliate.

RATING: 9
Hot damn, Star Realms is a tight, taut, humming ball of awesome.

It has almost none of the ramp-up that's typical of so many deckbuilders: You're buying pretty amazing shit right away, and then a turn or two later you're carving off your opponent's Authority in great swaths. It has that knife-fight-in-a-phone-booth feel that I love in Neuroshima Hex!, but packed into a pure deckbuilder; that's fantastic.

The whole game is just so well executed, with gorgeous artwork, clear card iconography and text, a microscopic rules footprint, smooth gameplay, so much delicious tension, a short play time, and a shockingly low price. Sleeve it, pack it all into a bi-fold deck box, and bam! Instant awesome in a tiny package.

RATING: 7.5
Deck building 101. If you've never played a deck builder and don't want to dive straight into the deep end (this was my first deck builder), this is a good gateway game. Building a good deck requires skill and some knowledge of the available cards, but the low complexity also means that luck plays a large factor in terms of both which cards come out in the display area and which cards you deal yourself with each reshuffle of your deck.  That makes the game great for imbalanced opponents--newbies won't necessarily get crushed as long as they thoroughly understand the basics.

Once played a few times the base deck feels a bit plain since there aren't that many special ability types (those beyond the basic attack, get currency to buy cards, and get life points back).  Expansion card packs are coming that promise to shake things up with events, personages, etc., so a solid base game could become more interesting and lively in time.  We'll see.

Pretty artwork, which never hurts.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[microbadge=1714]  [microbadge=21390]

02-Jul-2015:  This is a 2P deckbuilder with options for solo play.  The difference with this one is that you are attacking your opponent (reducing their score) rather than trying to gain VPs.  There are four factions in the game that are all played in conjunction with each other.  However, playing multiple cards of the same faction will provide extra actions or bonuses of some kind.  It's all about comboing those cards and building a powerful deck faster than your opponent.  It plays super fast and is very portable.

As for the solo game, there are two main options, both of which can be found online or in one of the expansion packs.  I have found the Pirates to be very random in terms of the difficulty level.  But the Nemesis Beast is pretty consistent.  Both solo variants play well enough though and provide a good sense of the game for the player.  However, a fellow solo gamer (ShaunGamer) has set up a wonderful set of solo challenges ([geeklist=189611][/geeklist]), including new AI enemies.  Wow!  You have to love the level of commit found around here.  It's just great.

So when taking into account all of these options, I'm very happy to have this little game in my collection.

RATING: 4
The good stuff first...it's inexpensive, portable, easy to learn, quick to play, visually clean and attractive.

But as far as the design and balance of the game, it's REALLY lazy, and doesn't do nearly enough to reduce the element of luck.  Every deck builder has an element of luck, but in Star Realms, once players understand basic stuff, every game is basically a coin flip.  That's not to say streaks won't happen, but it won't be the result of discovering a new layer to the game and elevating your play...or, conversely, having a strategy that your opponent effectively counters...it will just be luck.   

Luck is most influential early in the game, before the first shuffle.  In probably half of games, one player, through pure luck, has a significantly better hand after their first two turns.  It then becomes statistically probable that they are able to utilize that advantage before the second shuffle, and further increase their advantage.  So even though the role of luck tapers off over time, it just means that having the edge in luck early on is hugely more important than at any other time in the game.  

In the digital version, I've finished as high as #55 on the monthly leaderboard, so I think I understand the game on a deep enough level to say how shallow it is.  I would say 80% of my wins were pure luck, 17.5% were my opponents obviously buying the wrong cards, and maybe 2.5% were at least somewhat the result of doing something somewhat clever.

As an introduction to deck building, maybe it's okay, but even then I think I'd rather just teach someone Dominion.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
An awesome and very entertaining game! It has some elements from Dominion, but its game mechanics and theme make it a much better game than Dominion in my humble opinion and that of so many other people who consider this game their favourite deck builder. In order to win you have to make your opponent lose all his authority or in other words health, so you basically need to defeat him by force. You can purchase many different kinds of spaceships and bases which help you to fulfil your objective. These cards either give you some kind of ability, give damage to your opponent's authority, give you some more authority or grant you more resources to buy even stronger cards. Besides building your own deck and battling with your opponent, the game is pretty short and makes for an amusing little game to play. I cannot recommend it enough! It offers you so much joy and fun for such a tiny little compact box - if you can even call it a box - which can be stored away easily or brought along when traveling! It's one of the top three best deck building games I've ever played, if not my favourite! Don't think - just get it!

RATING: 7
Very fast, very interactive, very fun deckbuilder.  All the "races" seem fairly balanced and poses some critical choices between getting the 'best card' in the trade row vs. a good card which will have a higher likelihood of triggering synergies with other cards you are running in your deck.  There are some cards which are nearly game-winning in and of themselves (early Brainworld or Fleet HQ), and the game seems to escalate a little too quickly (doing 5-10 damage in one 'round' to 40+ the next).  Had a physical copy but gifted it, still have the full digital game and will probably get another physical copy.

What was I thinking?! Not only is this from the guy who designed Ascension, but comparisons to Ascension are everywhere...[i]and rate Ascension a 1.[/i] How could I be surprised when, playing this, I had a sense of deja vu, mindlessly acquiring green cards for a landslide victory in my first game. I'll admit there are probably nuances to card selection that I don't get in my first game, but there's an even greater gaping hole in the tactics for this game. RATING: 7.5
I'm disappointed by the unimaginative world-building--seriously, there's one faction called the Blob???--but it's sharp, fast, and full of agonizing choices. You don't want to dilute your deck with many disparate faction cards, but you still often need to grab cards from your opponent's factions to prevent them from getting too strong (or use the Blob's trade-row scrapping powers).

The price tag and physical size of the game vs the depth and replayability ratio is pretty damn amazing, incidentally. Best investment since Hanabi.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Another Deck Building Game but this one is pretty great. Plays in really short time and has that Ascension feel albeit in space. What separates it though is the fact that you need to wipe out your enemy HQ to win so direct attacking is possible. 

From that point of view think MtG meets Ascension but without the collectible element. 

As well as acquiring ships, the players can also acquire Bases, some of which protect your HQ as they must be attacked first - these are called Outposts.

Bases are also valuable because they stay in play between turns and continue to offer their benefits. 

Like certain expansions for Ascension some cards gain additional benefits when like cards are played - this is called Alliances. With only 4 factions in the game to date some really powerful combos can be created. 

Star Realms is easy to learn, fast to play and a come from behind combo is always on the cards. Great stuff.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Now I think I've overplayed the basic game, but 35 plays for that kind of money...

RATING: 6
A punchy little card game for 2 players that does deckbuilding well.  Star Realms understands the 2 main focuses of deckbuilding: Purchases that allow players to increase their hands size by drawing additional cards, and purchases that allow players to remove obsolete cards from their decks.

Fast, intuitive, artistically gorgeous, yet graphically easy to understand. Simple to introduce to new players and fun to play.

A word of warning: If, for some reason, you are having difficulty getting people to play SR, do not just get the App. Its not that the App is bad, on the contrary, you'll play the hell out of it and burn out on SR playing against the AI. The physical card game will just wind up gathering dust.

I will never, ever do this again. I got into board gaming to get away from the solitaire nature of video games, not to play them solo.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
This game is based on the type of deck building mechanics you see in games like Dominion. You start with a bunch of tame cards and buy from a central market and then attack your opponent. If you buy the same colour of cards, you get a bonus, and then you win or lose. 

This game could have used some serious editing and critical review prior to release. With a lack of inventiveness amongst the mechanics being matched by the lack of writing cleverness on the cards. For example, 'the blob' is the alien species and "the federation" are more human looking ships? That sort of derivative feel permeates the game and draws down any excitement I would have had about counting numbers on cards. 

This game also reminds me of several online card playing games where you attacked a castle based on random cards that were shuffled your way. If you were lucky enough to buy the blue ship in turn 1 and then find a blue base in turn 3, you will have activated the powers of that ship by pure chance. 

About the only mechanic I like about this game over Dominion is the market where cards are somewhat random. However, that idea has been used before in Machi Koro Harbor Expansion and other games.

Here's the thing: I like deck building games! This one just leaves me cold and numb counting yellow money an red attack symbols as if I was exposed to the harsh cold vacuum of space after my commander decides to scrap my perfectly good Explorer for +2 damage...

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I got "Star Realms" in February of 2015 after seeing it repeatedly on "Best Games of 2014" lists, and although I'm not big into card-building games, if I only owned one, this is it, as, once past the underwritten rules, it becomes an addictive adventure that manages to evoke its theme.

The rules sheet, a tri-fold, 3-panel color piece which unfortunately uses reverse white type, is underwritten, in that it lay out the rules but doesn't completely explain how the game is to be played. In that regard, the video by Gordon Werner is excellent for showing how to play: once I watched that I was able to go back to the printed rules sheet and make more sense of it. 

I also own the Fleets & Fortresses and Bases & a few other add-on sets, although one could get by on the base set, as within the original release there is much room for replayability. 

I'm a real sucker for space battle games and have own a few ("Light Speed" "Star Wars: Epic Duels," "Star Wars: X-Wing Fighther," "Star Borders--Humanity" "Space Station Assault," and "Mag Blast - 3rd Ed.") but this one feels like the most complete realization of the theme, especially with its relatively low level of complexity and how quickly one can play the cards to a decision. Repeated plays not only allows one to speed up one's play, it lets the inherent brilliance of the card interactions shine through.  Simply put, once you have the rules down, it's just plain fun to play.

心得:
基本上就是對打版的暗殺神
節奏很輕快，也比較不會有沒事做的狀況
還不錯玩
牌推分成四種陣營
藍色主要是購買能力加強跟補寫
紅色主要是把牌移出遊戲來壓縮牌庫，有張8防的base應該算是遊戲最強牌
黃色特色是棄對方手牌，一張四防的濾牌base也蠻強的
綠色主要就是數據都特別大，不論是買或攻擊或抽牌能力
策略面主要是看一開始中央牌庫狀況分析，能的話進量購買同種顏色牌
但是有強牌也是要優先購買
因為有移出遊戲加強能力的牌是使用上策略最大的地方
其它就剩下抽牌的運氣了
規則:
DBG 抽五張
base可一直保留
打爆進棄牌區

RATING: 8
I absolutely love this game.  Game play is very easy to learn and follows the strategy of most deck-building games.  My kids (ages 10-16) had no problems picking it up, and they love to play the game against me. I also have the Android game, and at any time I'll have 15 games going.  Quick turns, even as you think through the moves. 

There's more than just "get rid of the cheap cards" (although that's a key piece) to the strategy as similar types of cards leverage each other.  With the right hand you can absolutely devastate an opponent, although it takes several turns before you can get that far.

The downside for me is the general same one - the luck of the draw can play a deciding factor in winning or losing.

RATING: 7
It's a nice two player deckbuilder, with the traditional two currency format - gold for buying new cards from the shared draft (Legendary/Ascension style) and attack value. Instead of killing monsters, you're inflicting damage directly on your opponent. All the same pros and cons of Ascension ... getting lucky on whether synergistic cards are available to buy helps a lot, as does deck thinners. I mark it down a little because of the simplicity of the direct conflict (there's one less decision point on what to attack each turn) but others will mark it higher for that. It's fast, the rules are easy. The pool of available cards is probably a little limited for extensive replay, but I'm sure that the inevitable expansions will fix that.

RATING: 7
"This is an odd one, because I have probably played Star Realms 500 times on my phone. I really enjoy it there, because it is quick, but meaty, and I can either play the co-op scenario or just beat up on the AI. 

In real life, fiddling with cards makes the game take longer. I don't enjoy fighting against a human, and although I appreciate that there is a co-op mode, it's not great. 

So as a phone app, it's an 8 or 9. As an analog game in its normal competitive mode, it's a 4. Analog co-op probably gets a 6."

When I played Race for the Galaxy (against the AI) 500 times, I enjoyed it less and less, and ended up quite disliking the game. With Star Realms, it has held up very well to 500 plays against the AI, and if anything I enjoy it a bit more now than when I started.

RATING: 6.5
Based on one play: A deck builder with 5 cards available to buy at all times, which are shared between the players and just refilled as bought. Use your money cards to buy them and then mainly use them to attack the opponent (or his ships) and his bases. Kill the opponent to win. It works fairly well and since the cards are shared and visible it is easy to figure out what to do without being at too much of a disadvantage to someone who has played before.

RATING: 5
Probably the only deck building game I would choose to play.  Not a big fan of the mechanic, but this game was fun and quick for a few plays.  Then the typical issues I have with deck building started creeping in. The main downside for me is that the starting hand is just so dull. I realize that the purpose of the game is to build an awesome deck as the game progresses, but those boring initial 10 cards prevents me from wanting to start the game. With Star Realms, even the "cool" cards seem to lose their luster rather quickly.  Luck of the draw in this system can be frustrating when you purchase a mediocre card exposing an awesome card for your opponent.  Lucky shuffles can also result in 20+ or even 30+ plus hit point rounds that are just impossible to defend.  From now on, I will just play Race instead.

RATING: 10
This game was an unexpected hit with my girlfriend. I thought she didn't like scifi, but she looks past the theme and enjoys the gameplay a lot. She always tends to get the "Target opponenent discards a card" Star Empire cards! Each game is different. Making decisions and combining cards and developing a strategy as you play is SO FUN. Each game is so different. At first you might think you're going for Blobs only ... but then no more show up. So you buy some Machine Cult and then all there is is Trade Federation and Star Empire for several turns! Love this game. It's interesting how some cards that pop up in the Trade Row become "must-haves" and each player gasps and scrambles for them. Favourite card: Stealth Needle. 
Favourite faction combo: Blob/Trade Federation. Thematically, it feels like "The Trade Federation has had problems with the Blobs for at least 150 years, starting with the great Vorlon Uprising, and lasting to only a few decades ago. Recently, The Blobs have relented in their isolation and border protection, allowing safe passage through their domain to the Machine Cult territory beyond - in exchange for early Trade Federation technology."

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Star Realms was apparently the hotness of Origins 2014, and I can see why. Anyone who has played Ascension (or many other standard deck-builders) can learn this in about 12.7 seconds. It features head-to-head play, which makes it much more interactive than most of its predecessors. That’s a good thing, as a player must be constantly mindful of what her opponent is up to. 

I think no one would argue that the game has much depth. Strategies are very straightforward generally, and the game is mostly tactical. Even though, it plays in twenty minutes and there are enough interesting decisions during that time to hold my attention. And at 15 bucks retail, the game is a great buy. I wish the card quality was a bit better (I love me some linen finish), and despite what I’ve heard several reviewers say, the artwork is just average to me. Nevertheless, I can see this getting a lot of plays down the road. I’m sure it will be endlessly expanded, but I’m not sure I need additional content for now.

RATING: 6
It feels like Dominion base set - but in space & with battle damage.

The first few rounds are back and forth, clawing for something with good hit points or purchase value and discarding low-value cards to build a lean & powerful deck. But within 5 or 6 exchanges, the action kicks in and its a great little engine builder. Actually, I prefer it to base Dominion because the battle is so interactive that it never feels like two-player solitaire. 

I can't think of a better quick deck-builder filler. First time we played it, we played three times. 

Downsides /Caveats: The rule book can be a little vague in that it doesn't call out some things specifically, such as the difference between "played" and "in play" which affects, for instance, the timing of bonus effects and when the primary effects of bases & battle-stations kick in, and whether cards drawn as the result of a primary or bonus action can be immediately placed into play and affect other cards already played. Much of the action in later rounds of play hinges on concatenating bonuses from bases & outposts previously put into play, as well as primary and bonus actions from in-hand cards that allow drawing / discarding / trashing cards. Sometimes the difference between hitting for 8 damage or 18 damage depends on how you interpret the timing of the card interactions. If there's an exhaustive rules guide - please point me at it. :-)

RATING: 7
Star realms is a fun and very well balanced 2 player deckbuilding game where players draft from a center array of cards that refills when a player buys a card. There are cards belonging to different factions, which combo with each other in interesting ways. The wonderful thing is you can't win by building just one color. There are a number of different interesting strategies and combinations you can make.

:star: Art, Iconography and components functional
:star: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:star: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:star: Multiple plays stay interesting
:star: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::star::nostar::nostar::nostar: Raw enjoyment

RATING: 6
I like the simplicity of this deckbuilder.  I like that the game plays quickly, and I think the art on the cards is very well done.  My wife really enjoys this game.  My biggest gripe, however, is that I feel like it is almost entirely based on luck.  Luck of the draw with the cards in your hand, the luck of when a powerful, high value card becomes available for purchase in relation to when you have several credits to spend, and luck of whether or not more cards of the same faction will become available for purchase on your subsequent turns. There have been a few games that I have played that came down to the wire, but the majority of the games are determined very early on based on which player is able to obtain the most powerful cards in the beginning of the game.

Ugly, hugely imbalanced and luck dependant.

Overrated at best. Flawed. Not worth the time or investment.

RATING: 5
Star Realms is a well designed game... but lacks the spark that makes me want to bring it to the table.
As far as deck-builders go, this is a good one.   It's simple, streamlined, fits in your pocket, and is well balanced.  
And yet... it just isn't very exciting.  You'll never be on the edge of your seat, or spend nights thinking about new strategies to try next time.  There are never difficult decisions to make, and there is always a most optimal way to spend the resources you draw.   It kind of just plays itself in a way.

If you're looking for a good deck-builder, you won't be disappointed.
If you're looking for a great deck-builder, go track down Arctic Scavengers.

RATING: 8.87
Great 2P deck builder.  Less set-up than Dominion since you don't put out 10 small decks, you just lay out 5 cards from one big deck that both players can buy from.

The goal of the game is more intuitive (and thematic) than Dominion:  Kill your opponent (not sure why they used the word "authority").

On your turn you use your cards to either "buy" or "heal" or "attack".

The free app is very well done and absolutely addicting.  If I tracked the number of plays I had on the app, I would be embarrassed to provide the number because it would be so big.  :)

RATING: 6
I think this game would be great IF luck wasn't so important - especially in the first few rounds. If your opponent draws ships that are especially useful very early in the game (Freighter, Trade Pod, strong card-scrappers etc) and you don't, your chance of winning plummets. And that happens frequently.

A quick read of the forum suggests that the best-ranked Star Realms players win maybe fewer than two-thirds of their games, despite playing a wide range of opponents. This supports the idea that luck is a big factor.

The 'Gambits' expansion makes that problem worse. It gives players two random one-use special abilities, some of which help lucky players to acquire heavy-hitting, expensive cards very early in the game - giving them a big advantage.

The other problem is that the huge synergy between certain kinds of cards can make decisions about what to acquire/play quite obvious. So you might find the game both fiddly and like it is 'playing itself'. I often end the game and feel like a lucky early start decided the win, not good play.

That's a shame because the rest of the game has a good flow. Elements like bases, synergy and card-scrapping perhaps work better here than in its competitor Ascension. 

It is enjoyable trying to choose the best time to switch from growing your economy to bringing the enemy down. You can't leave it too late. If your opponent immediately has a good economic start, you might try to go all-out-attack from the beginning before they expand too much ... as long as the right ships are available (a few cheap Blob fighters get the ball rolling). That's the real fun of the game.

RATING: 9
My wife and I are often on the lookout for very portable two player games with a large amount of replay value. Star Realms seems to fit that bill quite well. We picked it up four days ago and have played about eight games since then. We've played a lot of Ascension, so the deckbuilding style of play and many of the mechanics were easy to learn. The game is a lot of fun, and as each game progresses into the mid-to-late game, there tend to be a lot of big punches and swings. It can be fun to see all your different allied cards triggering off of each other, enabling you to put together a very powerful turn. It's still early days, so I imagine it could be possible that at a certain point some particularly strong strategies or cards might make for repetitive experiences. But as of now, I'd say that it has a high replay value, and hard to beat for its price, fun, and portability.

RATING: 6
the good:  gorgeous artwork, interesting mechanics, fast play, solid implementation of deck-building, converts well to digital app

the bad:
Once the game gets going, the bame is balanced.  But there is a huge amount of luck in what cards you manage to get very early, which can create one-sided snowballs.

Without any expansion, the game can get a bit repetitive.  After a while, you recognize the viable strategies and executing a strategy feels the same every time.  Seeing blob-based decks work yet again is not interesting or enriched by context.

Getting locked out by a discard deck, base-destroyer deck, or simply being run over when nothing good is available on your turn can sour the game pretty quickly.

the main interaction is destruction fo bases, but there isn't actually much choice there.  You should almost always destroy based when your hand allows, so that point of interaction becomes random rather than reactive.

as a two player tabletop game: 5/10
as a one player digital app: 7/10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
An awesome little 2-player deckbuilder that streamlines and improves on the Ascension system.  Instead of maximizing endgame points, players are directly attacking each other, trying to reduce their opponent's life (Authority) to zero.  Think Ascension meets Magic the Gathering.  

Star Realms adds a lot of little tweaks to the gameplay that makes it really shine as a deckbuilder.  Many cards have Ally abilities, which allows multiple cards from the same faction to chain together to get bonus effects, rewarding a thematic, culled deck.  Other cards have Scrap abilities, in which permanently discarding them leads to other bonuses.  This results in some interesting gameplay decisions, as you determine how long to hang onto certain cards, or gain their bonuses while culling them.  

Another great introduction is the Bases, cards that stay in play until they are destroyed, and grant their own ongoing effects, or can support their faction with Ally abilities.  Some of these Bases are Outposts, which must be destroyed before Authority can be diminished.  These are a great element to the game, and allow for different strategies.  

The factions are interesting and feel different from each other, whether you try to make an efficient deck with the Machine Cult, reduce your opponent's hand while increasing your own with the Star Empire, or choose all-out warfare with The Blob.  The artwork is great and evocative of the sci-fi theme.

All of this gameplay comes in a tiny deck box with about 100 cards.  This is a super fun game, plays very quickly (about 20 minutes), and allows for both deckbuilding and direct player interaction (through combat!).  I can't wait to play this some more, top marks.  Also looking forward to the expansions.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I found playing the electronic version very addictive. AS deck-builders went, it felt like one of the better ones. Face to face was OK, but I increasingly got the feeling that the pool of cards you have on your turn to choose from had a too large role in determining victory. The game came to feel a tad repetative. The small expansions I tried in the elctronic version didn't seem to add much. As a filler, though, the game certainly has its place. It is certainly more than worth it's small price, as the initial exploration of the system is very enjoyable.

RATING: 9.5

I have played is Android free version vs AI over 100 times and never got bored. I liked this game a lot and prefer it over Dominion.

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Structured very closely to the Ascension series, Star Realms attempts to carve its niche with cards which do more for less, and a more streamlined objective of taking your opponent down. For the most part this works in Star Realms' favour, as the game feels faster paced and simpler to approach. The game can escalate very quickly in power though, and poor luck or choices early in the game can quickly snowball. Also, Star Realms leaves little room for clever card play, but expansions should fix that shortly. In the end though, Star Realms and Ascension tread the similar line of being games that straddle between managing the chaos of luck and making worthwhile decisions.

Unlike Ascension, Star Realms is a strictly 2P game right out of the box. This doesn't really pose a problem, since I always thought Ascension plays best with 2P anyway. I have yet to try Star Realms as a multiplayer game, and don't intend to any time soon.

I was unimpressed with this one upon first play- perhaps it was just too overhyped. But as we played more, it started to grow on me. Then, I picked up the app and thoroughly enjoyed playing through the campaign and have played against the bot hundreds of times. Deckbuilding is the integral mechanic to this quick, hard-hitting game. I really feel the need to consider each one of my purchases- will this card work well with my deck or just be in the way? It takes a little bit to get your feet off the ground, but eventually you can build up some satisfying combos. And a complete game only takes about 20 minutes! It's pure genius.

RATING: 5
Can't really understand the overall top ratings. I'm a big fan of Ascension BUT I don't like Star Realms! (which seems unlikely) I played it for 50+ games (digital version), but it never really got me hooked. For me the differences are frustrating:
-Bases/Outposts (like the Constructs in Ascension) have normally a short durability so I'm getting no engine-building feeling (growing stronger is more fun!)
-a lot more dependency of card drawing luck (in Ascension combining different 'colors'->Factions is not a bad thing)
-cards don't give you VPs so there is no need for buying cards at the end and only hoping for a good draw to smash the opponent
-the game could end unexpected because your opponent with a worse deck has a perfect draw -> lower control
-don't like the trashing function of cards, I don't want to trash good cards (especially expensive Bases/Outposts; why there is no discard-function instead?)
-you cannot beat some campain-games without a perfect card display (no fun!)
Overall I have no feeling of controlling the game even if I win. I stay with Ascension and Dominion for sure. A bit sad, I wanted to like Star Realms. :(

RATING: 8
Great game. Fast, fun, most decisions are obvious, but it has just the right amount of decision making. 
The illustrations are quite nice too look at, if a bit dark. The different factions and their unique abilities make it possible to play a focused deck, but it is not necessary. Random distribution means of cards that there is no killer combo strategy, which makes for an interesting change of pace. I find this to be right in between Ascension, which is much more chaotic, and Dominion, which is muss less chaotic.
In only a few weeks of time, this became one of my most played games ever.

RATING: 7
In a world glut with deck builders, Star Realms has firmly carved out it's niche as the go-to quick filler deck builder. While I would never plan an night of Star Realms, it's definitely on the top of the list of games I'd look to if I needed to fill a 15-20 minute span.

The mechanics are very similar to say, DC Deckbuilder or Ascension, but Star Realms fits them into a small, tactical, fun little package.

I also enjoy the head-to-head aspect, rather then who can accumulate the most points.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Not as good as Puzzle Strike, because despite constant attacks there isn't really meaningful interaction as in how your opponent is playing will influence how you play mid-game -- it still feels like pick a strategy, race to [-]gain VP[/-] deal damage, with the added thing from Ascension of making tactical buy decisions with a changing-bank. But dealing 26 damage to your opponent in one turn is sitll a hell of a lot more satisfying than buying three provinces.

Initially, I'm not sure how balanced the game systems are, I think not very. There seem to be a few different strategies and interactions, but ultimately trashing + attacking seems very powerful and very safe compared to the others which rely heavily on luck -- future buys are unknown, making long-game play risky, and there are few ways to mitigage luck in the game (where combos are super powerful and you want to draw certain cards together) other than trashing and drawing. No way to save cards/stagger draws, no way to play cards from discard pile, etc. However, in theory, if the right cards come up, the healing and discard cards seem like great counters to trashers/attackers, just less reliable.

I expect it'll more likely just be a satisfying filler at most.

[b]I'd Rather Play:[/b] Puzzle Strike

(3 Design + 2 Enjoyment)

RATING: 6
Fantastic game. Quick, fun, tense and very cheap. What more can you ask for? The best filler for 2 players by far. This is the only deckbuilder I like actually (have not tried that many though). It just captures the essence of what's great about deckbuilders. At least to me. In 15 min playtime you get a good amount of player interaction and the excitment of the card draws from both your own deck and the 'shared' trade row is definitely there. The space theme is perfect and the different factions all have a distinct feel to them.

Yes, some turns are on auto pilot but there is usally still a lot of difficult choices you'll have to make over the course of a game. There is definitely a lot of luck in the game too, a good deal more than Dominion for sure and while I don't necessarily see this as a bad thing it does prevent me from rating it a pure 10. For now :) 

Lowered my rating by 1 because they didn't include two more sets of starting Scouts and Vipers. That forces me to buy a new copy of the basegame to play 3-4 players. Money grab

Lowered my rating by 2 because the pricing of the expansions. I have to pay more for fewer cards? Really? "Ohh don't worry, we've packed the expansions into 4 separate booster packs. 4x5$ instead of 1x20$ FEELS much cheaper!" BS and money grab.

RATING: 7
2 plays

A cool little card game that feels like a blend of Magic (different colors with individual strengths; attack the other player to reduce their life to 0) and Dominion (deck-building on the fly). My first game wasn't much fun: seemed almost totally random, sort of like a variant of Ascension -- with it's constantly changing card display and double currency -- but set in space and with direct player-on-player combat. The next one, though, was much better: we both paid closer attention to the cards, drafted a different combo of factions, and tried to play to our deck strengths. Fun!

[b]--- Summary ---[/b]
Players each start with the same deck: 8 money ("trade") and 2 combat; shuffle and draw a hand of 5. The deck of cards, with a mix of bases and ships, is shuffled and 5 are put in a face up display. A pile of Explorers (2 trade, 2 combat) is also placed in display. Players each start with 50 life ("authority").

On your turn, play your cards for their currencies and/or effects:
- Trade is used to buy cards from the display (replenished immediately): buy as many as you can afford; cards go to discard pile.
- Combat is used to attack your opponent and/or their base(s); if they have "outpost" bases, you must attack them first. Destroyed bases go to that player's discard pile. Split your total combat value as needed to make as many attacks as possible.
- Ships and Bases provide a variety of effects. Many have additional effects if you have another card of that faction in display. Some can also be trashed ("scrapped" = removed from game) for additional effects. These effects tend to split along faction lines. One of them specializes in deck-thinning abilities, another in card-drawing, a third has powerful attack, and the fourth provides a lot of trade.

At the end of your turn, your Bases remain in play; other cards, played or still in hand, are discarded.

The first player to reduce opponent's life ("authority") to 0 wins!

RATING: 5
This is essentially just Ascension with a space theme.  It is a deckbuilder with a central row of cards and players take turns playing the cards from their hand to accumulate two resources, attack and buy power, and acquire cards from the center row to make their deck stronger.  Pretty much the only difference between this game and Ascension besides the theme is that attack power is used to directly hit your opponent instead of monsters in the center row and the winner is the person who drains the opponent's life total.  Even that really just is a minor difference from attacking monsters in the center row to accumulate victory points.

Even many of the factions serve identical roles to Ascension.  The red faction in this game (I forget the name) has cards that provide a lot of attack power and trash cards from your hand and discard pile, just like the purple faction from Ascension (I forget that name as well).

With it essentially being the same game unless you really dislike the theme from Ascension or really like the theme from this game there's really no reason to play this over Ascension.  Ascension has been around a lot longer and has a lot of additional content.  This game just came out and will get boring pretty quickly seeing the same cards over and over again.  Even comparing base sets this game is a lot more boring, none of the cards do anything exciting, just the same stuff with different numbers.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
I've played this on my tablet/phone before so I had a good idea of what it was going in. The app has a few things going for it like instant shuffling, instant setup and asymmetric play.  The card game of course has human interaction and the other benefits of being a physical game.

The game is very random, strategy really doesn't seem to play much part in the game, you may luck into 3 or 4 cards of the same faction and steamroll your opponent because of it, or you may never see a combo and just never really take off. The game is very swingy and random but to an extent that's okay.

If I'm looking for a fast light deckbuilder type game this fits the bill but it's more a filler than a "lets play this all night" type game for me.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Simple, streamlined version of the Ascension-style deck builder. Very fun. Still suffers from the same problem that other Ascension-style deck builders have. ie: If one player misses out on getting really good cards early game because their one of their opponents grabbed it, they tend to suffer from it for the rest of the game. In particular, players who grab scrappers/exilers (cards that exile cards from the user's hand/deck; used for improving efficiency) early game whilst also depriving their opponents of opportunities to grab them, then they just win. The good thing is that the cards are designed in such a way that even in those situations it's not completely one-sided and the other player can still make a solid recovery and eventually a comeback.

Also, the battle system just works great. One of the main issues with deck builders is that during mid/late-game peoples' turns just take forever because they're getting off like a million consecutive draw card effects so they just take forever with their turns. Star Realms' battle system fixes that effectively because once any player does that, the game is most likely over, or at least soon to be over.

Definitely should only be played 1v1 or team v. team.

RATING: 8.2
Overall: Deep competitive tactical game with cool theme. 8.2/10

Gameplay: This is where the game shines. Although this card is fairly luck dependent, but like poker, the better player will always have a higher win percentage in the long run. Like poker, this game will appeal to both casual and competitive gamers. The card draft mechanic is neat and a game can be easily finished in 30mins. 

Components: Artwork is simple and crisp, but can turn off certain gamers. Everything fits nicely in a small portable box. For those that prefer fantasy, there is Hero Realms, which is mechanically similar fantasy themed game. 

Replay value: This game has so many expansions, the replay value is quite high. Since games are quite short, one game is never enough.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Light deckbuilding game that for once eschews "get points" for "crush your enemies, see them driven before you, and to hear the lamentations of their civil partners of any gender".

Cards provide either coins (buy stuff) or attack (attack the enemy). The key differentiator is the faction bonus system, whereby most cards have a bonus effect which activates if another card of the same colour was out in the same turn. Some cards, representing planets and space stations, can be played for persistent benefits (as well as permanently assisting with faction bonuses); if they are "outposts" then they must be destroyed before the player can be hurt.

You know, sometimes I feel like the bar has really been lowered by the proliferation of deckbuilding games post-Dominion. More than most of its competitors, Star Realms is obviously derived from Magic: the Gathering, and its limitations -- the almost total lack of strategy beyond, the feeble excuse for engine-building -- help highlight just how good traditional constructed-deck games are.

Star Realms has peppy pacing and is sufficiently different from the current crop to feel like a breath of fresh air, but scratch the surface and it's just as constrained and dull in slightly different ways. Not a patch on Ascension, or even on other non-entirely-successful attempts at Magic distillation, like Small Box Games' Tooth & Nail: Factions.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Update again:  Crap!  This game is amazing!  My son had 1 point left. One!  I had 26.  In one crushing turn, he flipped a ton of cards  and blasted me out of the universe.  He was jumping around, fists in the air.  I am looking for expansion.  10+

Update:  Playing with my 8 year old son.  He keeps asking to play.  He's absolutely in love with the game.  

(No experience with Ascension or Magic) Superb!  I've never had a game up and running so quickly by simply reading the rules.  Fast, fluid, fun, filler-ish, with fighting factions, and even, a times, a bit frenetic.  Great for those who are frugal.  $15!  

On a personal note, I almost didn't buy this because my wife does NOT like confrontational games.  That this game is entirely based on wiping out your opponent did not bode well.  However, she was able to pick it up quickly after we played a starter 30 point game.  She was annoyed and a bit antagonistic at first--especially after an initial loss.  However, she quickly realized she had been too non-confrontational and attacked heavily, in the second 50 point game, and won! 

This game is about as smooth a game as I have run into.  The card design is intuitive and tactical plans are very easy to formulate.   The factions make strategic planning a breeze.  As an added bonus, the card art is deeply impressive.

I had only wanted a 2 player game.  I'm considering another pack now.  

Looking forward to expansions.  Wow!

RATING: 9
Logged hundreds of plays on the digital version. I think it deserves a 9 just for that.

==Not for you if==

Luck does play a role. Apart from getting unlucky with your hand, taking a card from the trade row can potentially reveal a good card for your opponent who happens to have the right cards to take that card.

Very rarely, the outcome might be decided on the first few  turns just because one of the players was very unlucky and the other one was very lucky (assuming that both players have a certain level of understanding of the game and won't make obvious mistakes later on).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This game has been a great deck builder on the go. It is small and there is enough balance for a decent two player experience. It is nice to have the option to go more than two, though I haven't tried it. Unique to the deck building genre, it uses a direct attack against the other players to get a win, whereas, typically, the deck building genre ranges from cooperation to a more euro-style victory-point-grab.

The only negative I have is the cards supplied for keeping score. They could be a bit more intuitive. With one number on the front and a different one on the back, the player has to think a moment how best to use the cards to merely track your remaining "authority" points. There have been games that we have had to use alternate means because the requisite cards were not available to keep score. 

Altogether, not a bad buy. The game was very affordable when I bought it at $15 and if you spend an hour and half playing it you have spent what you would've on a movie for you and a friend.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Logging all Star Realms plays here, even for Colony Wars games.

What a great game. Besides my grail deckbuilders, Dominion and Core Worlds, this is probably the best I've played. It's certainly a lot better than the other "draft cards that are drawn randomly from a deck" deckbuilders such as DC, Legendary, and Ascension. This game basically fixes all of the problems with those games by making all of the cards useful. No longer do you have to build your deck around one certain type of cards, because pretty much every card is useful to some degree. 

I just wish there were a multiplayer version. Sure, there are rules for playing with more than two players, but it seems like that just wouldn't work because of the attacking. In free-for-all, weaker players could just get eliminated right away. In the "attack your left-hand neighbor" mode, the weakest player would give a disadvantage to his neighbor.

Still, this is one of the best two-player deckbuilders out there.

RATING: 8
Rating is based on initial play, I've tried it 4 player so far.

So, this game is like the clarion call to anyone who doesn't like deckbuilders for the "solo" aspect.  There is heavy multi-player action here, along with fun mechanics and some nice artwork.

The game largely centers around getting faction combos, and has a "river" of available cards, similar to Ascension.  The gameplay seems well balanced, with the ability to get life back and setup outposts that act as shields.

Overall, a REALLY fun first experience, and I expect this to become a staple for a long time.  Bravo!

RATING: 7
Star Realms is a two player deck building game where you directly attack the other player.  It's a little surprising that it took this long for somebody to come out with a direct combat deck builder.  There are a couple of new interesting ideas in Star Realms, namely ally bonuses when cards of the same faction come out on a turn, and the ability to permanently trash cards for a one-off bonus.

Nothing in Star Realms is extraordinarily revolutionary or groundbreaking, but it does what it does very well.  It's a very enjoyable quick deck building game.  I can see this game getting a good bit of play with my son and myself.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
[thing=147020][/thing] is a deck building that comes in a tiny box ([thing=463][/thing] early style) whose cards won't ever, ever return to the box if they are sleeved (I know this isn't a big issue for players US, but it is important around here, in Brazil, due to the big expanse that is shipping and taxes).

[thing=147020][/thing] uses mechanics akin to those of [thing=69789][/thing] and [thing=129437][/thing]. Basically the deal is this: each player uses trade (money) do acquire spaceships and stations/bases, and uses them to attack the other opponent (lowering his Authority value). Spaceships are cards that you use and then discards; stations, once you put them on the table, they stay there for use in later turns - the bases can be attacked by your opponent, so that you lose the constant benefit of them (but this, of course, protects your Authority value, since the attack is mostly used against the station).

The cards avaialble for purchase stay in a track in the middle of the table, and after one or more of them are bought, new ones come from the main deck - it is the same as what happens in [thing=69789][/thing], in [thing=125678][/thing] and in [thing=129437][/thing], and has the same issue of all of those: you may have a high value of trade in your turn and not a pretty good card to buy (though, from what I saw, most of the cards are, at least, useful, but there might not be an excellent card that fits into what you really want to get accordingly to your strategy in the game), and you buy one or other just because, and then an awesome card shows up for your opponent to take. The biggest advantage of this way of dealing with the cards is that the set up is a breeze (just shuffle everything and you are good to go), and also you have, prior to playing, less explanation to do (you just have to look and explain those five that showed up - but you do will have to lose time reading the effects of new cards most of the time).

In a similar way to what happens in [thing=129437][/thing], [thing=147020][/thing] makes cards from the same faction to combine among themselves, creating lots of synergies when cards of the same faction are played in the same turn (and the bases really help this to happen, since they stay on the table and count as a "played" card in every turn they are there), and this works great to make some cards with lower value be actually sought after for a player, since it goes with the others he already purchased earlier, which makes the "always buy the most expensive one" have way less of an effect in the gameplay decisions.

There are fours factions in the base game: the Trade Federation, the Blobs, the Star Empire and the Machine Cult. Each faction has somewhat of a focus, which makes them different from each other: the Trade Federation generates more trade value and Authority (this is basically your "life points"); the Blobs makes some powerful attacks; the Star Empire also have some good attacks, but also makes you draw more cards and your opponent to discard; and the Machine Cult, besides being an awesome metal band name, also has strong bases and allows the player to get rid of unwanted cards, taken them out of your deck.

One unique thing for [thing=147020][/thing] is that most of its cards has a trash effect: you get a one time bonus, and the card goes to the box, out of your deck. Since, as expected, the trash effects are pretty good, you usually are torn between use it or keep the card for later uses. This not only works great to give the players more options, while also makes deck-thinning less important (in [thing=36218][/thing] and several others, for instance, deck-thinning is usually the best way to go, so it is nice to see a change of pace).

The purpose of the game is to make your opponent Authority to reach zero (each player starts with 50). Some bases have a key-name: Outpost, and these your opponent must attack first before attacking you directly (so, for instance, say you have two Outposts in the table, one with value 5 and the other 4, and your opponent gives a total attack of 12 - in this case, the two Outposts would be destroyed (going into your discard pile) and your Authority value would be lowered by 3 points (the excess of damage). It is important that the Outpost defense value must be overcome in a single attack, because damage is not marked on the card nor it stays from one turn to the other (so, if a Outpost has a 5 value, and your attack has a strength of 4, you won't to a thing to the Outpost). The winner will be the first player to reduce the other player's Authority to zero.

Those that know my taste in games are aware that confrontational games for two players aren't really my thing - I don't really like to conflict directly to someone else (in a multiplayer game, this is majorly lessened because of the presence of other players, thus the "head to head" fight, even when it occurs, I don't really mind). So, what I think of [thing=147020][/thing]? Surprisingly enough, I did like it, quite a bit, actually. The gameplay is agile, dynamic, the turns play fast and the synergies are interesting to pull off. The art is great! A game quickly becomes is a constant of massive attacks, so the 50 Authority points don't last much once they start to fade. Even when I was learning the game, a whole play didn't last longer than 20 minutes!

At this point I can't avaluate if the overall game tends more to the side of great plays or the side of the luck into taking the best and more unique cards, since because all different the cards come from the main deck, it is possible that one powerful card only shows up once (even if it has multiple copies of it in the deck). Also, one can invest in a faction, only to see cards for it coming in droplets. I couldn't see a way to avoid or control this (there are cards that let you scrap cards from the central row, usually one you don't want your opponent to take, and this can accelerate the main deck in order for it to spill some good cards for you, but you can't be sure of this - you can take out a great card for your opponent just to put an amazing one in its place). However, some degree of luck will always be a part of any deck builder (it's a feature, not a bug), but those that work with a central track for the purchase of cards tend to suffer more in this matter - and in my play, even though it was furious and fun, it wasn't balanced: the result was 25 to -13 (yes, negative 13).

Anyway, I liked what I saw and what I played, and it was possible to see why [thing=147020][/thing] is receiving so much attention lately: I wanted to play again right after the end of the game (and [thing=147020][/thing] intends exactly this: repeated plays, since experienced players can surely have 3 or 4 matches in one hour). So, is [thing=147020][/thing] approved? Yes, it is!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I have played Star Realms over 100 times and since I now know the cards and patterns it is easy to tell which side is going to win within the first 3-4 turns.  In a nutshell this is a fast paced light damage game where the winner is often the player who stumbles on the resources needed to buy the best toys first then steamrollers to victory.  Star Realms is the type of game where you need to play it a bunch of times to mitigate random losses due to the above so it really works better as an app.  Recommended for tweens to early teens as a gateway game for future real deck building (like MtG) endeavours and a mental distraction while squeezing out a long crap.  Star Realms is worth the app price but not more than $10 for a physical copy.

That's ok, it has a place.  And I like that place sometimes...

RATING: 8
Star Realms is a highly portable, streamlined, quick to play deckbuilder that was an instant hit with my friends, my girlfriend and my 12 year old son. Everyone just wanted to play again right after their first play. Soon after my first couple of games, I sold Ascension. The games are too similar for me to keep both in my collection, and it is more fun to try and eliminate your opponent than it is to obtain the most victory points. The only thing that bugged me about Ascension, is that each card has a vp value, making deck thinning less appealing than in Star Realms. Best with two, even though attacking your left neighbour while defending against attacks from the right works fine as well.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
As crazy as this sounds, this replaced Dominion for me. What I'm after in a pure deck builder is quick set up time, quick playing time and just none of the headaches that so many deck builders seem to fall victim to. 

It does lose the intricacies where the closer you are to winning, the harder it becomes to win as you simply need to reduce your opponent's life total to 0 and don't have victory point cards clogging up your deck. At the same time, not having to count and see who won at the end is a plus. 

Quick, fun, fantastic!

Update 01/03/15 - Dropped from a 8 to a 6. There are more issues; I wonder if big money is just disguised in this game and thus less of a problem. The random flip can be incredibly frustrating (even though games can be quite fast). It's still enjoyable but I think I may be bored of it. The expansions seem to lead to longer games which detracts from what made Star Realms so great.

Update 10/04/15 - Dropped from a 6 to a 4. This is just marginally better than Dominion for the length it takes. Otherwise forget it; being on the receiving end because your opponent just has perfect money and the perfect card flips after you buy is one of the worst feelings in gaming. The AI in the ios app is a rampant cheater. I've lost count of the number of times where I've bought a 8 cost base, only to end up dying before drawing it, and it being in the last 5 cards of my deck.

Update 22/05/15 - Dropped from a 4 to a 3. I can't even play the iOS app anymore due to the amount of cheating the AI employs. It's maddening; to the point where it feels like the game is playing me.

Update 19/06/15 - Dropped to a 1. I'm never going to play this game again.

Update 02/07/15 - Upgraded back to a 5. This is fine once in a while, and only with the expansions. At least the random events and the new cards make this a little fresher. I refuse to play just the base game or the app with just the base game. I know the events aren't everyone's cup of tea, but this game is already incredibly high variance, you can't really take this all too seriously.

Expansions:
Crsis - Bases & Battleships
Crisis - Events
Crisis - Fleets & Fortresses
Crisis - Heroes
Star Realms: Colony Wars

RATING: 6.9
Current pinnacle of the deck-building scene. Easy to learn but offers great many approaches and variance. Faction abilities, card mechanics and general rules are well laid and the usual deck-building ills of solitaire play (this game is truly interactive with punches taken and received left and right) and trailing are almost completely gone from this. Tracking authority is a bit clumsy but past that very little to improve here. By far the best #-building game you can shop for. Affordable as well. Can't really help that these never managed to set me ablaze.

A second thought after too many matches played:
The choice in this game is an illusion. You play all your cards from hand every round and shop from a limited pool of cards that actually narrows down the more you play (as winning tactics dictate what you buy). The game pretty much plays itself and after a while I am starting to bore as audience.
